==PAGE:3==
Preface
This master thesis is written as partial fulﬁllment of the requirements to obtain a M.Sc.
degree in Biomedical Engineering at the Technical University of Denmark. The thesis is
a cooperation between two students, Lena M. Nilsson and Maria E. L. Sakhat, the De-
partment of Health Technology at the Technical University of Denmark, Bispebjerg and
Frederiksberg Hospital and Rigshospitalet. It undertakes the building of an algorithm for
detecting ventricular tachycardia and ventricular ﬁbrillation for a clinical support system
for the Wireless Assessment of Respiratory and circulatory Distress project. A statement
of contributions can be seen in Appendix F, where a table indicates the primary contrib-
utor of each section in the thesis. The thesis period is from August 2020 to February 2021,
and represents a workload of 30 ECTS per student.
Lena Mahler Nilsson and Maria Emilie Loft Sakhat
Copenhagen, February 2021

==PAGE:7==
Abstract
Introduction: Early warning scores are the current standard for monitoring hospitalized
patients. These are measured at ﬁxed intervals up to 12 hours apart, possibly leading to
fatal events going unnoticed and a slower reaction time for the medical staff. The Wire-
less Assessment of Respiratory and circulatory Distress (WARD) project is a new clinical
24-hour monitoring support system using small non-intrusive wireless wear-and-forget
devices coupled with intelligent algorithms. Hereby, the medical staff can continuously
monitor patients and detect otherwise missed events, for instance a ventricular ﬁbrillation
(VF) episode. VF is often preceded by ventricular tachycardia (VT), followed by sudden
cardiac death, and is present in 70% [40] of cardiac arrest patients. This project develops
a VT/VF detection algorithm for the WARD system.
Methods: A VT/VF algorithm is developed on public databases, with the VFPred al-
gorithm by Ibtehaz et al. (2019) [30] implemented as a reference algorithm and used
as a starting point. Initially, a data segmentation into overlapping 5-second segments
is performed, followed by preprocessing, a feature extraction with an empirical mode
decomposition, discrete Fourier transform and cosine similarity measure, a ReliefF fea-
ture selection algorithm, a synthetic minority over-sampling technique to overcome class
imbalance, and lastly a classiﬁcation with a non-linear support vector machine, evalu-
ated with relevant performance measures. Performance optimization is attempted and
various classiﬁcation thresholds are used to select two optimal models, one found using
a Euclidean distance measure, while another selected for WARD implementation. This
selected model is then veriﬁed on other public databases, and ﬁnally trained on and ad-
justed for WARD data, concluded by evaluation, and then veriﬁcation by medical experts.
Results: For the optimal VT/VF algorithm trained on public databases, a sensitivity of
96.73%, speciﬁcity of 97.48%, accuracy of 97.10% and positive predictive value of 97.46%
is found. For the optimal VT/VF algorithm for WARD implementation, a sensitivity of
85.31%, speciﬁcity of 99.84%, accuracy of 92.58% and positive predictive value of 99.81%
is found. For the VT/VF algorithm trained on WARD data, a sensitivity of 63.29%, speci-
ﬁcity of 81.49%, accuracy of 72.39% and positive predictive value of 77.37% is found.
Conclusion: The veriﬁcations show that the VT/VF algorithm on public databases per-
forms excellently and competes with state of the art, while the performance of the VT/VF
algorithm trained on WARD data is not ideal and results in many false alarms. However,
it was optimistic to expect the algorithm to be implementable within the time scope of
this project, and it should be considered research in progress, to be continued. Ultimately,
the rare occurrence of VT/VF episodes creates a very difﬁcult classiﬁcation problem.
iii
==PAGE:8==
Resumé
Introduktion: Early warning scores er den aktuelle standard for monitorering af indlagte
patienter. De måles ved fastlagte intervaller, med op til 12 timer imellem. Herved kan
fatale episoder overses, og resultere i længere reaktionstid for det medicinske personale.
Wireless Assessment of Respiratory and circulatory Distress (WARD) projektet er et nyt,
klinisk, 24-timers monitoreringssystem, der anvender små, ikke-invasive, trådløse, wear-
and-forget apparater koblet med intelligente algoritmer. Da kan medicinsk personale
monitorere patienterne kontinuerligt og opdage de ellers missede episoder, for eksempel
episoder af ventrikelﬂimmer (VF). Ventrikeltakykardi (VT) kommer ofte før VF, mens VF
ofte er efterfulgt af pludselig hjertedød og forekommer i 70% [40] af hjertestoppatienter.
Dette projekt udvikler en VT/VF detektionsalgoritme til WARD systemet.
Metoder: En VT/VF algoritme er udviklet på offentlige databaser, hvor VFPred algorit-
men af Ibtehaz et al. (2019) [30] er implementeret som referencealgoritme og brugt som
udgangspunkt. Først er data opdelt i overlappende 5-sekunders segmenter, efterfulgt
af præprocessering, feature-ekstraktion med en empirical mode decomposition, diskret
Fourier transformation og cosinus-similaritet, en ReliefF feature-udvælgelsesalgoritme,
en syntetisk minoritets-oversamplingsteknik for at reducere klasseubalance og endelig
en klassiﬁkation med en ulineær support vector machine, evalueret med forskellige præ-
stationsmål. Præstationsoptimering er forsøgt og forskellige klassiﬁkationsgrænseværdi-
er er brugt til at udvælge to optimale modeller, den ene fundet ved Euclidisk afstand og
den anden udvalgt til WARD implementering. Denne udvalgte model er da veriﬁceret
på andre offentlige databaser og endelig trænet på og justeret til WARD data. Slutteligt
en evaluering og da veriﬁcering af medicinske eksperter.
Resultater: For den optimale VT/VF algoritme trænet på offentlige databaser ﬁndes en
sensitivitet på 96.73%, speciﬁcitet på 97.48%, nøjagtighed på 97.10% og positiv prædik-
tiv værdi på 97.46%. For den optimale VT/VF algoritme til WARD ﬁndes en sensitivitet
på 85.31%, speciﬁcitet på 99.84%, nøjagtighed på 92.58% og positiv prædiktiv værdi på
99.81%. For VT/VF algoritmen trænet på WARD data ﬁndes en sensitivitet på 63.29%,
speciﬁcitet på 81.49%, nøjagtighed på 72.39% og positiv prædiktiv værdi på 77.37%.
Konklusion: Veriﬁceringerne viser, at VT/VF algoritmen på offentlige databaser præste-
rer fremragende og måler sig med state of the art, mens præstationen for VT/VF algorit-
men trænet på WARD data ikke er ideel og resulterer i mange falske alarmer. Dog var det
optimistisk at forvente at algoritmen ville være implementérbar indenfor dette projekts
tidshorisont, og det skal anses som forskning undervejs, til senere fortsættelse. Ultimativt
udgør den sjældne forekomst af VT/VF episoder et meget svært klassiﬁkationsproblem.

correctly classiﬁed and red for misclassiﬁed. The bottom plots show each
individual segment, green for correctly classiﬁed and red for misclassiﬁed. .
82
7.13 Example of a misclassiﬁcation from Model 3 of the VT/VF algorithm. Four
segments are classiﬁed as non-VT/VF, but are in fact VT/VF, correspond-
ing to a false negative (FN), while the surrounding segments are correctly
classiﬁed as VT/VF, corresponding to a true positive (TP). The top plot
shows the signal with dashed boxes indicating each segment, green for
correctly classiﬁed and red for misclassiﬁed. The bottom plots show each
individual segment, green for correctly classiﬁed and red for misclassiﬁed. .
83
7.14 Example of a misclassiﬁcation from Model 3 of the VT/VF algorithm. Three
segments are classiﬁed as non-VT/VF, but are in fact VT/VF, correspond-
ing to a false negative (FN), while the surrounding segments are correctly
classiﬁed as VT/VF, corresponding to a true positive (TP). The top plot
shows the signal with dashed boxes indicating each segment, green for
correctly classiﬁed and red for misclassiﬁed. The bottom plots show each
individual segment, green for correctly classiﬁed and red for misclassiﬁed. .
83

1. Introduction
This chapter provides an overview of the motivation behind this project, the problem
deﬁnition, the project goals as well as the project structure.
1.1
Motivation
Currently, hospitalized patients are monitored by the medical staff using early warning
scores at ﬁxed intervals, which can be up to 12 hours apart. Early warning scores is a scor-
ing system based on six vital signs determining the degree of illness of the patient based
on the score for each vital sign. [56] This can lead to important or possibly fatal events [14]
going unnoticed or being noticed late, resulting in a slower reaction time for the medical
staff. When the general state of the patient is poor, these unnoticed events can later lead
to complications. Since these patients are connected to several machines, their ability to
move around and comfort is likewise limited. This problem is particularly prominent in
post-surgery and chronic obstructive pulmonary disease patients, for which the idea for
the Wireless Assessment of Respiratory and Circulatory Distress (WARD) project origi-
nates. Worldwide, more than 200 million [26] patients undergo major surgery each year,
with 30% [26] of these experiencing severe complications within the ﬁrst 30 days.
The WARD project is a new clinical monitoring support system providing 24-hour mon-
itoring via small non-intrusive wireless devices called wear-and-forget devices, coupled
with intelligent algorithms. This allows the medical staff to monitor the state of a pa-
tient during all hours of the day, and detect these mentioned otherwise missed events. In
addition, physiological measures that are usually not measured on the given ward can
now be measured using these devices. This includes for instance the electrocardiogram,
which measures the electrical activity of the heart, which usually is limited to the cardiol-
ogy ward of the hospital. By measuring this continuously, events such as life-threatening
arrhythmias, which require immediate action, can be detected promptly. By use of a
real-time alarm, the medical staff is immediately notiﬁed of any relevant decline in the
condition of the post-surgery or chronic obstructive pulmonary disease patient, enabling
them to react quickly and thereby increasing the treatment efﬁciency. [68]
As this project expands, the WARD clinical support system becomes useful for additional
hospital wards, including both surgical and medical patients. Some of these wards do
not require the patients to be hooked up to machines, and the WARD wear-and-forget
devices do not counter this state, but allow the patient to move around freely, creating as
little inconvenience as possible for the patient, while gaining much more thorough infor-
1
==PAGE:27==
1.2. PROBLEM DEFINITION
Introduction
mation about their state at all times. [68]
Cardiovascular disease is the leading cause of death worldwide. In 2016, approximately
17.9 million [73] people died from cardiovascular diseases, corresponding to 31% [73]
of all global deaths.
Of these, 85% [73] are due to heart attacks and strokes.
Many
of these cardiovascular diseases can evolve into cardiac arrhythmias and subsequently
death. One of the most prominent antecedents of sudden cardiac death is ventricular
ﬁbrillation, which is present in nearly 70% [40] of cardiac arrest patients, often preceded
by ventricular tachycardia. The reaction time when faced with ventricular ﬁbrillation is
crucial for the patient’s chances of survival and recovery, as merely minutes without re-
action can prove fatal. Speciﬁcally, the rate of survival declines at a rate of 5-10% [40]
for every minute without deﬁbrillation. This is one of the fundamental reasons for de-
veloping a ventricular tachycardia and ventricular ﬁbrillation detection algorithm for the
WARD clinical support system, which is the main goal of this project. Enabling continu-
ous monitoring via the WARD system can allow the medical staff to react much quicker
than otherwise, which can be life-saving in the best case and life-changing in any case.
Incorporating a high-performing, real-time ventricular tachycardia and ventricular ﬁbril-
lation detection algorithm in a continuous monitoring system such as WARD would be
an achievement of great value to patients and medical staff.
1.2
Problem Deﬁnition
This project aims to develop a ventricular tachycardia and ventricular ﬁbrillation detec-
tion algorithm for a clinical support system known as WARD. WARD performs contin-
uous monitoring of postoperative patients and chronic obstructive pulmonary disease
patients among others, based on small wireless wearable sensors coupled with intelligent
algorithms. This developed classiﬁcation algorithm should be able to excellently detect
when a ventricular tachycardia or ventricular ﬁbrillation event occurs, and hereby be
used to alarm the medical staff of this critical condition. To avoid an abundance of false
alarms, the speciﬁcity and the positive predictive value should be considered as the most
relevant performance measures.
To produce an algorithm for the WARD clinical support system, a ventricular tachycardia
and ventricular ﬁbrillation detection model must be developed on public databases to
ensure comparability with other developed algorithms, and then trained on and adjusted
for WARD data. Lastly, an evaluation is conducted along with a veriﬁcation by medical
experts to estimate the performance of the algorithm implemented in the WARD system.
1.3
Project Goals
The project goals include the following points:
• Conduct a literature study to determine the state of the art of ventricular ﬁbrillation
and/or ventricular tachycardia detection algorithms
2
==PAGE:28==
1.4. PROJECT STRUCTURE
Introduction
• Implement a reference algorithm to test reproducibility and provide grounds for
comparison
• Develop an algorithm to detect ventricular tachycardia and ventricular ﬁbrillation
using public databases
• Optimize the algorithm developed using public databases
• Find the optimal algorithm developed using public databases and select an optimal
algorithm with regards to implementation in the WARD system
• Verify the algorithm developed using public databases with intended use in the
WARD system on other public databases
• Acquire annotations on data from WARD arrhythmia study by aligning LifeTouch
ECG recordings with Holter ECG recordings
• Develop an algorithm to detect ventricular tachycardia and ventricular ﬁbrillation
using LifeTouch ECG recordings
• Optimize the algorithm developed using LifeTouch ECG recordings
• Test the algorithm developed using LifeTouch ECG recordings on data from the
WARD project
• Verify the performance of the algorithm with the help of independent medical ex-
perts
• Ensure convertibility of the ﬁnal algorithm from MATLAB to C++
• Discuss the beneﬁts and drawbacks of this type of algorithm in the WARD system
• Write a draft of an article for submission to the 43rd Annual International Conference
of the IEEE Engineering in Medicine and Biology Society
1.4
Project Structure
The project goals are fulﬁlled with the following elements of the thesis.
The chapter "Wireless Assesment of Respiratory and Circulatory Distress Project" pro-
vides information about the WARD project that surrounds this master thesis.
The chapter "Physiological Background" provides insight into the physiological back-
ground for ventricular tachycardia and ventricular ﬁbrillation related to the heart, elec-
trocardiogram and arrhythmias.
The chapter "State of the Art" describes the conducted literature study and subsequent ref-
erence algorithm implementation. Speciﬁcally, small summaries of selected top articles
3
==PAGE:29==
1.4. PROJECT STRUCTURE
Introduction
within the ﬁeld are presented, in order to create an overview of the existing algorithms to
detect ventricular ﬁbrillation and/or ventricular tachycardia.
The chapter "VT/VF Algorithm" presents the methods used in the development of the
ventricular tachycardia and ventricular ﬁbrillation detection algorithm. Namely, the de-
scription of included ECG data from public databases, the data segmentation process as
well as the methods used for respectively preprocessing, feature extraction, feature se-
lection and classiﬁcation is described. The obtained performance is then attempted to be
optimized and lastly, the developed algorithm is veriﬁed using other public databases.
The chapter "VT/VF Algorithm for WARD System" presents the methods used in the de-
velopment of the ventricular tachycardia and ventricular ﬁbrillation detection algorithm
for the WARD clinical support system. Speciﬁcally, the description of ECG data from the
WARD arrhythmia study, the data alignment process, the data segmentation procedure
as well as the data processing including the methods used for respectively preprocessing,
feature extraction, feature selection and classiﬁcation are described. The developed algo-
rithm is then veriﬁed by medical experts using WARD data.
The chapter "Results" provides the obtained results from the reference algorithm imple-
mentation, the ventricular tachycardia and ventricular ﬁbrillation detection algorithm us-
ing public databases as well as the ventricular tachycardia and ventricular ﬁbrillation de-
tection algorithm using WARD data for the WARD clinical support system.
The chapter "Discussion" discusses the obtained results including the beneﬁts and draw-
backs of the developed algorithms as well as which factors may inﬂuence the perfor-
mances. In addition, suggestions for future work are made.
The chapter "Conclusion" concludes the thesis and fulﬁllment of project goals by summa-
rizing the vital results, namely the performances of the developed algorithms and main
discussion points.

2. Wireless Assessment of Respiratory and
Circulatory Distress Project
A description of the Wireless Assessment of Respiratory and Circulatory Distress project,
that is the premise behind this project, is provided in this chapter.
2.1
Aim of the WARD Project
Worldwide, more than 200 million [26] patients undergo major surgery each year, with
30% [26] of these experiencing severe complications within the ﬁrst 30 days. These com-
plications may be asymptomatic and therefore go undetected, resulting in post-surgery
complications being a prominent cause of death for these patients. [14] This presence of
undetected complications not only has consequences for the patients, but also leads to
high costs for the hospital and government, speciﬁcally in Denmark, where each severe
complication costs more than 100.000 DKK. When the complications occur without warn-
ing while the patient is not monitored, their severity may be unnecessarily increased. [26]
The Wireless Assessment of Respiratory and Circulatory Distress (WARD) project is a
new clinical monitoring support system, aiming to become the next generation monitor-
ing system for high-risk patients, so far including post-surgery patients, patients hospi-
talized with chronic obstructive pulmonary disease (COPD) and patients with COVID-
19, which results in a reduction of complications as well as health-care expenses. This
project is constantly expanding to include additional patient groups and diseases. [26]
[68] It was founded in 2016 by MSc, PhD, Associate Professor Helge B. D. Sørensen from
DTU as well as MD, DMSc, Associate Professor Eske K. Aasvang from Rigshospitalet
and MD, PhD, Associate Professor Christian S. Meyhoff from Bispebjerg and Frederiks-
berg Hospital and is supported by the Innovation Fund Denmark, Danish Regions, the
Novo Nordisk Foundation, the Danish Cancer Society, the Steno Diabetes Centres Den-
mark, Copenhagen Center for Health Technology, Bispebjerg and Frederiksberg Hospital,
DTU Health Tech, Rigshospitalet, Radiometer, Isansys and the A. P. Møller Foundation.
[67] The contribution from the Novo Nordisk Foundation is speciﬁcally intended for the
monitoring of COVID-19 patients. [7]
5
==PAGE:31==
2.2. BACKGROUND OF THE WARD PROJECT
Wireless Assessment of Respiratory
and Circulatory Distress Project
2.2
Background of the WARD Project
Hospitalized patients are currently monitored at ﬁxed intervals by the medical staff, which
can be up to 12 hours apart. Since these patients are connected to several monitoring ma-
chines, their ability to move around as well as their comfort is limited. The degree of
illness of a speciﬁc patient can be determined using early warning scores (EWS), which
is a scoring system based on six vital signs. These vital signs include the respiration rate,
oxygen saturation, systolic blood pressure, pulse rate, level of consciousness and temper-
ature. For each physiological measure, a score typically ranging from 0 to 10 is given,
illustrating the deviation of the measurement from the norm. [56] Here, a score from 0-4
corresponds to a low clinical risk, a score from 5-6 corresponds to a medium clinical risk
while a score from 7-10 corresponds to a high clinical risk. [48]
Using EWS, patients are only monitored at ﬁxed intervals, meaning events can go unno-
ticed or be noticed late between these intervals. Hereby, a 24-hour monitoring would be
very beneﬁcial, as a continuous monitoring can increase the treatment efﬁciency of the
patient among other things. The difference between the monitoring at ﬁxed intervals and
the continuous monitoring is illustrated in Figure 2.1. This is the fundamental idea be-
hind the WARD clinical support system, which provides 24-hour monitoring via small
non-intrusive wireless devices, known as wear-and-forget devices, coupled with intelli-
gent algorithms. This concept also allows the patient to move around freely, rather than
conﬁned to the monitoring machines. Thereby, as little inconvenience as possible is cre-
ated for the patient, while much more thorough information about the state of the patient
can be collected at all times. [26] [68]
Today 
Future  
Figure 2.1: Illustration of the current ﬁxed interval monitoring with EWS and the new continuous
monitoring with the WARD system. Modiﬁed from [26].

ing manufacturer and measured physiological parameters. [24]

2.3.1
LifeTouch Blue ECG Sensor
The LifeTouch Blue ECG sensor from Isansys is a small wireless device continuously mea-
suring single-lead ECG. Hereby, the heart rate, respiration rate and heart rate variability
of the patient can be calculated. The device has two button electrodes with the sensor

located at the thickest end, which is placed in the 4th intercostal space, approximately 2

angle below the chest. The ECG sensor placed on the chest of a patient can be seen in
Figure 2.3. [31] [69]

2.4. WARD SYSTEM PROCESS
Wireless Assessment of Respiratory
and Circulatory Distress Project
As seen in Figure 2.4, wear-and-forget devices initially measure physiological parame-
ters. These measurements are sent via Bluetooth to a gateway tablet, which forwards the
signals to an approved server using WiFi. From the server, the physiological parameters
are sent to the medical staff via a smartphone application known as WARD 24/7 Clinical
Support System (CSS) App. The medical staff is assigned to a number of patients and the
application provides an overview of these, including a summary of the vital signs of each
patient. This screen can be seen in Figure 2.5. [26] [68]
Figure 2.5: Summary of the vital signs of each patient on the smartphone application WARD 24/7
CSS App. [70]
For each patient, three icons are visible representing the connection of the devices, their
battery levels and activated alarms as seen in Figure 2.5. Here, a green color indicates a
steady connection, high battery level and no activated alarms whereas a red color indi-
cates no connection, low battery level and activated alarms. If the patient exceeds one of
the predeﬁned thresholds due to a complication, the alarm icon turns red and a real-time
alarm is triggered alerting the medical staff. An alarm can also be activated if a device is
disconnected or if their battery level is low. The predeﬁned thresholds for different types
of events are presented in Table 2.2. [70]
Table 2.2: Overview of the predeﬁned thresholds triggering a real-time alarm on the smartphone

3. Physiological Background
An overview of the anatomy and physiology of the heart is presented in this chapter,
followed by a description of the relevant cardiac arrhythmias.
3.1
The Heart
The heart is described by respectively its anatomical structure and its electrical activity
including the electrocardiogram.
3.1.1
Anatomical Structure of the Heart
One of the fundamental requirements for life is a pumping heart. The heart is an essential
muscular organ located in the thoracic cavity between the lungs, with the main purpose
of pumping blood through the body via the circulatory system. The anatomy of the heart
can be seen in Figure 3.1. [65]
Figure 3.1: Anatomical model of the heart. [19]
As seen in Figure 3.1, the heart consists of four chambers, namely the right and left atria
as well as the right and left ventricles. The heart also has four valves ensuring a one-way
ﬂow of blood, in addition to large blood vessels, carrying blood to and from the heart.
The anatomical placement of these can be seen in Figure 3.2. There are two atrioventric-
ular (AV) valves, the tricuspid and the mitral valve, ensuring that the blood cannot ﬂow
back into the atria from the ventricles. The tricuspid valve is located between the right
atrium and the right ventricle, while the mitral valve is located between the left atrium
11
==PAGE:37==
3.1. THE HEART
Physiological Background
and the left ventricle. In addition, there are two semilunar valves, the pulmonary and the
aortic valve. The pulmonary valve is located between the right ventricle and the large
pulmonary artery, which leads to the lungs. The right side of the heart pumps blood to
the lungs, which returns oxygenated to the left side through blood vessels of the pul-
monary circulation. Finally, the aortic valve is located between the left ventricle and the
largest vessel, the aorta, which leads out into the systemic circulation. The left side of the
heart pumps blood to the tissues, which returns to the right side through vessels of the
systemic circulation. Hereby, the heart ensures the ﬂow of oxygen-rich blood to tissues.
The anatomical placement of the four heart valves as well as their opening and closing
patterns during respectively diastole and systole can be seen in Figure 3.2. [65]
Figure 3.2: Illustration of the anatomical placement of the four heart valves as well as their opening
and closing patterns during respectively diastole (a) and systole (b). Illustrated by a cross section
of the heart. [65]
When the heart muscle relaxes, known as diastole, the tricuspid and mitral valves open,
while the pulmonary and aortic valves close, allowing blood to move from the atria to
the ventricles, as illustrated in Figure 3.2a. When the heart muscle contracts, known as
systole, the opposite will occur, where the tricuspid and mitral valves close, and the pul-
monary and aortic valves open, allowing blood ﬂow out of the heart, which can also be
seen in Figure 3.2b. [65]
3.1.2
Electrical Activity of the Heart
The heart has its own individual conduction system, which is independent from neural
stimulation. Instead, specialized pacemaker cells initiate the contractions of the heart.
The conduction system of the heart consists of the sinoatrial (SA) node at the top of the
right atrium, the AV node located at the wall between the right atrium and ventricle,
right and left bundle branches as well as the Purkinje ﬁbers, which cover the entire ven-
tricle walls. This is illustrated in Figure 3.1. All conduction system cells in the heart have
the ability to produce spontaneous action potentials, but the primary pacemaker is the
SA node, which produces action potentials faster than the other cells, as well as sodium
12
==PAGE:38==
3.1. THE HEART
Physiological Background
and calcium channels opening and closing spontaneously at a rhythmic rate. The action
potentials travel from here to the AV node, which causes a small delay, while simulta-
neously spreading over the atria, activating their contraction. From the AV node, the
impulse travels through the left and right bundle branches and into the Purkinje ﬁbers,
spreading through the apex to the entirety of the ventricle walls, causing the simultane-
ous contraction of the ventricles. The delay in the AV node allows the atria to ﬁnish their
contraction before the ventricles start to contract. [65]
3.1.2.1
Electrocardiogram
To measure and visualize the electrical activity of the heart, an electrocardiogram (ECG)
is often used. Electrodes are placed on the skin, and will detect the electrical currents
generated by the heart. A pair of electrodes can constitute a lead, so in a typical diag-
nostic ECG, where 10 electrodes are used, 12 leads are formed. The electrical potential
difference between each pair of electrodes is what is measured as the depolarization or
repolarization of the heart occurs. During depolarization, the electrical wave will move
towards the electrodes and be recorded as positive, while the opposite occurs for repolar-
ization. The 12 leads typically used in a diagnostic ECG ensure that the electrical activity
of the heart is observed from several different planes. A visualization of an ECG from a
heart in normal sinus rhythm can be seen in Figure 3.3. [54]
Figure 3.3: Illustration of an electrocardiogram of a heart in normal sinus rhythm with indications
of the different peaks and waves. [36]
As visible from Figure 3.3, a heart beat on an ECG consists of different peaks and waves.
These are denoted P, Q, R, S and T. The P-wave corresponds to the depolarization of the
atria, while the QRS-complex, lasting from the beginning of the Q-wave to the end of the
S-wave, corresponds to the ventricular depolarization. Lastly, the T-wave corresponds to
ventricular repolarization. Repolarization of the atria is hidden in the QRS-complex, due
to overlap. [54]
13
==PAGE:39==
3.2. CARDIAC ARRHYTHMIAS
Physiological Background
3.2
Cardiac Arrhythmias
Cardiovascular disease is the leading cause of death worldwide. In 2016, approximately
17.9 million [73] people died from cardiovascular diseases, corresponding to 31% [73]
of all global deaths.
Of these, 85% [73] are due to heart attacks and strokes.
Many
of these cardiovascular diseases can evolve into cardiac arrhythmias and subsequently
death. Sudden cardiac death is often preceded by cardiac arrhythmias, which are dis-
orders of the cardiac rhythm and these can originate both in the atrial and ventricular
conduction system and surrounding muscle.
Cardiac arrhythmias can be generally divided into bradyarrhythmias and tachyarrhyth-
mias. Bradyarrhythmias are deﬁned as a slow heart rate, with the most common being
sinus bradycardia, presenting with a heart rate of less than 60 beats per minute (bpm). The
severity of the bradycardic condition depends on the duration and the following cardiac
output reduction. If the bradycardia is persistent, symptoms such as fatigue, shortness of
breath, exercise intolerance, dizziness and inability to concentrate are common. If instead
the bradycardia is intermittent, the most common symptoms will be due to the sudden
lack of cerebral perfusion, and present themselves as vertigo, blurred vision and syncope.
[71] Tachyarrhythmias are deﬁned as a fast heart rate, above 100 bpm, and can be either
supraventricular or ventricular. Supraventricular arrhythmias indicate an origin between
the SA node and the AV node and include a regular QRS-complex, while ventricular ar-
rhythmias originate after the AV node and include wide QRS-complexes. [4] An overview
of common cardiac arrhythmias, including the heart rate in bpm and ECG components
can be seen in Table 3.1.
Table 3.1: Overview of common cardiac arrhythmias, including the heart rate in bpm and ECG
components. [8] [17] [53] [71]
Arrhythmia

This project focuses on ventricular arrhythmias, speciﬁcally ventricular tachycardia and
ventricular ﬁbrillation as presented in Table 3.1. Ventricular ﬁbrillation is often preceded
by ventricular tachycardia and is present in nearly 70% [40] of cardiac arrest patients,
thereby one of the most prominent precedents of sudden cardiac death.
3.2.1
Ventricular Tachycardia
Ventricular tachycardia (VT) is a ventricular arrhythmia deﬁned by a heart rate above 100
bpm with no precedence of P-waves, therefore originating in the ventricles. The QRS-
complexes are more than 0.12 seconds wide. VT can be either monomorphic, where the
QRS-complexes look alike, or polymorphic, where there is a beat-to-beat change in QRS
morphology. [33] [53]
VT can be categorized into non-sustained and sustained VT. Non-sustained ventricu-
lar tachycardia (NSVT) is deﬁned as three or more consecutive ventricular extrasystoles
(VES) terminating by itself within 30 seconds. VES, also known as premature ventricu-
lar contractions (PVC) originate in the Purkinje ﬁbers in the ventricles and can result in
a reduced or no cardiac output. Common symptoms for NSVT are palpitations, chest
pain, shortness of breath, dizziness and fatigue. However, single VES are harmless and
do not require any intervention. Sustained ventricular tachycardia (SVT) is deﬁned as a
VT episode lasting more than 30 seconds. It can be associated with hypertension, and in
some cases, it can also last less than 30 seconds because of the need for intervention. Sus-
tained VT leads to a reduced ventricular ﬁlling and a reduced contraction force, meaning
a reduced cardiac output. This consequently means that the patient often will collapse,
and the condition may turn into ventricular ﬁbrillation. [17] [33] [53]
One of the underlying causes of VT is reentry, which is associated with tachycardia trig-
gered by scarring after a myocardial infarction.
Reentry is characterized by a circuit
within the myocardium including two pathways, one with a fast conduction velocity
and long refractory period, and one with a slow conduction velocity and short refractory
period, as is illustrated in Figure 3.4. [63]
15

Figure 3.4: Illustration of the reentry concept in 6 steps from (a) to (f). "SLOW" indicates a slow
conduction velocity and short refractory period pathway whereas "FAST" indicates a fast conduc-
tion velocity and long refractory period pathway. A black arrow indicates an action potential from
a normal beat, while a blue arrow indicates action potentials from a premature beat. Red shading
indicates a refractory period, and "X" indicates an action potential stopping its propagation.
When a normal action potential enters the pathway, as illustrated by the black arrow in
Figure 3.4a, it splits into the fast and slow conduction velocity pathways. As seen in Fig-
ure 3.4b, the action potential will travel faster in the fast conduction velocity pathway
and result in the refractory period, which is illustrated with red shading. The action po-
tential will split once more at the top branch, continuing to the rest of the heart, and the
"X" represents the collision of two action potential moving in opposite directions, which
cancels them out. When a premature beat action potential then enters the pathway, as is
illustrated by the blue arrow in Figure 3.4c, it splits just as the other action potentials do,
but the fast conduction velocity pathway is still affected by the refractory period, mean-
ing that the action potential cannot travel through here. However, the refractory period
in the slow conduction velocity pathway is shorter, and allows for the action potential
moving in this pathway to continue and split at the top branch, as is illustrated in Figure
3.4d. As the split occurs, the refractory period for the fast conduction velocity pathway
is over as well, allowing the action potential to continue into this pathway and continue
around in the loop as can be seen in Figure 3.4e, thus naturally resulting in another refrac-
16
==PAGE:42==
3.2. CARDIAC ARRHYTHMIAS
Physiological Background
tory period, as is apparent in Figure 3.4f. Additionally, in Figure 3.4f, it is seen how the
following normal action potential is stopped by the refractory period. This process will
continue, and the action potentials get stuck in a loop of continuously ﬁring and navigat-
ing between refractory periods, causing the VT. [63] Other causes of VT are heart failure,
effects of drugs or medications, electrolyte imbalance or valvular disease. [17]
The treatment of VT depends on the presented symptoms of the patient. In the event of
no pulse, the treatment option is similar to ventricular ﬁbrillation, namely a direct current
(DC) deﬁbrillation and cardiopulmonary resuscitation (CPR). However, in the event of a
pulse combined with a simultaneously unstable patient, a DC synchronized cardiover-
sion is required. Here, a low-energy shock is used compared to deﬁbrillation, which uses
a high-energy shock without the need of timing the electrical current. The DC synchro-
nized cardioversion should be synchronized at the QRS-complex, as a shock occurring
close to the T-wave can lead to ventricular ﬁbrillation. [17] [41]
An example of a segment of a VT episode from MIT-BIH vfdb [23] extracted from a raw
ECG signal can be seen in Figure 3.5.

3.2.2
Ventricular Fibrillation
Ventricular ﬁbrillation (VF) is characterized by chaotic oscillations rather than QRS-comp-
lexes. It is a ventricular arrhythmia with a heart rate above 200 bpm. This causes the ven-
tricles to quiver rather than contract, and no cardiac output is generated. Left untreated,
it is life-threatening, as this will result in sudden cardiac death. While the symptoms
are similar to asystole, some electrical activity is still present, enabling deﬁbrillation as
a treatment option. [17] The reaction time is crucial for the patient’s chances of survival
17
==PAGE:43==
3.2. CARDIAC ARRHYTHMIAS
Physiological Background
and recovery, as merely minutes without reaction can prove fatal. Speciﬁcally, the rate of
survival declines at a rate of 5-10% [40] for every minute without deﬁbrillation.
VF is caused by multiple localized areas of microreentry, which leads to no organized
electrical activity in the ventricles. [53] Other causes of VF can be hypoxia, electrolyte
imbalance, ventricular tachycardia and other arrhythmias, hypothermia or heart disease.
[17]

ECG signal can be seen in Figure 3.6.

4. State of the Art
A literature study is conducted to determine the state of the art within the studied ﬁeld.
Speciﬁcally, algorithms used to detect VT and/or VF are of interest. To ﬁnd the most
relevant articles for the literature study, a thorough search of articles is executed.
4.1
Search Methods
To ﬁnd the most relevant and innovative articles, a certain procedure is carried out. Dif-
ferent search engines are used, namely DTU Findit, IEEE Explore and ScienceDirect. The
search is then limited to journal articles and conference papers, and only articles pub-
lished between the years 2010 and 2020 are searched for, in order to obtain only the more
recent literature. Different combinations of keywords are used in the search for articles,
and these can be seen along with an overview of the previously mentioned search criteria
in Table 4.1.
Table 4.1: Overview of search criteria used for a state of the art literature study.

The searches conducted based on the criteria given in Table 4.1 result in a large amount of
hits. To ﬁnd the most relevant articles, the title is initially used to discard the less relevant
articles. Next, the abstract of a chosen article is read, and if this seems promising, the full
article is read. The found conference papers are discarded unless they display a quality
comparable to journal articles. In addition, articles are read if they are observed to be very
commonly referenced to, and possibly constitute the origin of an algorithm. To be noted,
these referenced articles can have a publication date before the year 2010. An overview
of the read articles can be seen in Appendix B, and the best articles are selected for close
reading.

In addition to the information provided in Table 4.2, a few other facts about each database
might be relevant:
• MIT-BIH afbd [43] includes ECG data chosen at random from a set of 4000 24-
hour ambulatory ECG recordings. The recordings have a resolution of 11-bit in a 10
mV range, and have been independently annotated by two or more cardiologists.
Annotations include ventricular ﬂutter, ventricular tachycardia, atrial ﬂutter, atrial
ﬁbrillation among others.
• MIT-BIH vfdb [23] includes only rhythm annotations, which mark changes in car-
diac rhythm. The annotated arrhythmias include sustained ventricular tachycardia,
ventricular ﬂutter and ventricular ﬁbrillation, atrial ﬁbrillation among others.
• MIT-BIH nsrdb [21] includes ECG data containing normal sinus rhythms meaning
no signiﬁcant cardiac arrhythmias are present.
20
==PAGE:46==
4.3. CHOSEN TOP ARTICLES
State of the Art
• CUDB [49] includes annotations of sustained ventricular tachycardia, ventricular
ﬁbrillation and atrial ﬁbrillation. All signals were passed through an active second-
order Bessel low-pass ﬁlter with a cutoff of 70 Hz. The recordings have a resolution
of 12-bit over a 10 V range.
• AHADB [13] includes eight arrhythmia categories. Only the last 30 mins of each
recording includes classiﬁed beats. The recordings have a resolution of 12-bit.
• PAFDB [45] consists of 50 training record sets and 50 test record sets. Here, approx-
imately half of the record sets in the test set come from subjects with paroxysmal
atrial ﬁbrillation (PAF). The data has a resolution of 16-bit.

VFPred: A fusion of signal processing and machine learning techniques in detecting
ventricular ﬁbrillation from ECG signals
Authors: Nabil Ibtehaz1, M. Saifur Rahman1 and M. Sohel Rahman1, 2019 [30]
1) Department of CSE, Bangladesh University of Engineering and Technology, Dhaka, Bangladesh
Introduction: This study presents an algorithm incorporating empirical mode decompo-
sition (EMD) and discrete Fourier transform (DFT) in combination with a random forest
feature selection. This is followed by a support vector machine (SVM) classiﬁer to distin-
guish between VF and non-VF.
Database(s): ECG data from CUDB and MIT-BIH vfdb is used. This respectively includes
35 records of 8-minute single-channel ECG data and 22 records of 30-minute single-channel
ECG data.
Methods: The VFPred algorithm initially performs preprocessing of the ECG signals.
Using EMD, the preprocessed signals are decomposed into a set of intrinsic mode func-
tions (IMFs) representing oscillatory functions. Based on a noise level crossing ratio and
a chosen constant, the appropriate IMF component and residue is then selected for fur-
ther analysis. These are resolved into frequency components via DFT and features are
computed. The most promising subset of features are identiﬁed through a random forest
algorithm. Using an SVM classiﬁer with a Gaussian radial basis function (RBF) as a ker-
nel along with a 10-fold cross-validation, the ECG events are classiﬁed as VF or non-VF.
The imbalance in the data set is overcome by using a synthetic minority over-sampling
technique (SMOTE) developed by Chawla et al. [9] and additionally accounted for using
a geometric mean (G-mean) accuracy measure. The process is illustrated in Figure 4.1.
1-channel
ECG
Moving-
average
ﬁlter
High-
pass
ﬁlter
Low-
pass
ﬁlter

Figure 4.1: Block diagram describing the algorithm introduced in Ibtehaz et al. (2019) [30].
Results: The algorithm obtained a sensitivity of 99.99%, a speciﬁcity of 98.4% and an ac-
curacy of 99.2% with a 5-second window length.
Comments: This study achieved excellent results. However, a portion of the data is used
as the training data and the rest as the validation data, meaning no separate test data is
made, which they ought to have to increase the reliability of their results. In addition,
annotated noise has been removed before the training of the model, which is not viable

in a real-time algorithm.
Machine Learning Approach to Predict Ventricular Fibrillation Based on QRS
Complex Shape
Authors: Getu Tadele Taye1, Eun Bo Shim2, Han-Jeong Hwang1 and Ki Moo Lim3, 2019 [61]
1) Department of Medical IT Convergence Engineering, Kumoh National Institute of Technology, Gumi,
South Korea, 2) Department of Mechanical & Biomedical Engineering, Kangwon National University,
Chuncheon, South Korea, 3) Department of IT Convergence Engineering, Kumoh National Institute of
Technology, Gumi, South Korea
Introduction: This study aims to predict episodes of VF 30 seconds before their occur-
rence, using features extracted from QRS-complexes.
Database(s): ECG data is used from CUDB, PAFDB and MIT-BIH nsrdb. Speciﬁcally, 28
records from the CUDB are used as a VF group, while 22 records from the PAFDB and 6
records from the MIT-BIH nsrdb are used as a control group.
Methods: Initially, each ECG signal is divided into required and forecast time. The re-
quired time spans from 150 to 30 seconds before the VF onset and is used for feature
extraction, while the forecast time spans the last 30 seconds before the VF onset. Next,
11 heart rate variability (HRV) features are computed for comparison purposes, while 4
QRS-complex features are computed in addition, speciﬁcally the mean and standard de-
viation of respectively the QRS-complex signed area and the R-peak amplitude. These
features are used to train two fully connected artiﬁcial neural networks (ANNs), one us-
ing the HRV features and one using the QRS features. The algorithms are evaluated 10
times using 10-fold cross-validation. To compare, SVM, k-nearest neighbour (KNN), ran-
dom forest and Gaussian naive Bayes algorithms are likewise implemented. Lastly, a
two tailed t-test for each model is used to evaluate differences between the VF and HRV
features. Additionally, one-way repeated-measures analysis of variance (ANOVA) with
Tukey post hoc analysis for multiple comparisons is used to compare the machine learn-
ing models. The process is described in Figure 4.2.

Ventricular Fibrillation and Tachycardia Classiﬁcation Using a Machine Learning
Approach
Authors: Qiao Li1,2, Cadathur Rajagopalan3 and Gari D. Clifford2, 2014 [39]
1) Institute of Biomedical Engineering, School of Medicine, Shandong University, Jinan, China & 2)
Department of Engineering Science, Institute of Biomedical Engineering, University of Oxford, Oxford,
UK & 3) Mindray DS USA, Mahwah, NJ, USA
Introduction: This study presents a VF classiﬁcation algorithm utilizing a machine learn-
ing method. The algorithm is based on 14 VF metrics and a genetic algorithm (GA) used
to select the features ensuring optimal performance when testing with an SVM classiﬁer.
Database(s): All records including VT and VF signals are included from CUDB, MIT-
BIH vfdb and AHADB. Respectively 35 records of 8-minute single-channel ECG data,
22 records of 35-minute two-channel ECG data and 10 records of 30-minute two-channel
ECG data, adding up to a total of 67 records of annotated ECG data.
Methods: The algorithm initially performs preprocessing of the ECG data to reduce base-
line wander, high-frequency noise and power line interference. Next, 14 VF metrics are
extracted using various methods. These are comprised of a complexity measure, a VF-
ﬁlter leakage measure, four spectral analysis features, a time delay measure, auxiliary
counts from a bandpass ﬁlter, a covariance measure, a frequency measure, an area mea-
sure and a kurtosis measure. Using a GA, a feature selection is performed. The clas-
siﬁcation is then performed using an SVM classiﬁer with a Gaussian radial basis func-
tion kernel, training the model using 5-second window length signals and every possible
combination of features, thereafter choosing the feature combinations with the best per-
formance. Lastly, the performance is evaluated on a validation set, cross validating over
selected feature combinations and window lengths. The process of the algorithm is illus-
trated in Figure 4.5.

is 96.4% while with a 5-second window length, an accuracy of 96.3%, sensitivity of 96.2%

parameters, which helps ensure that they have optimized their algorithm to the best
of their abilities. They thoroughly explain their chosen parameters, and obtain a good
performance for the classiﬁcation of VF. In addition to VF rhythms, they have included
non-VF rhythms to a great extent, indicating that their algorithm should be able to
classify VF even in the presence of other arrhythmias, and thereby distinguish between
them, which is a much desired trait for the purposes of this project.
Detection of ventricular tachycardia and ﬁbrillation using adaptive variational mode
decomposition and boosted-CART classiﬁer
Authors: Yang Xu1, Dong Wang1, Weigong Zhang1, Peng Ping1 and Lihang Feng1, 2018 [75]
1) School of Instrument Science Engineering, Southeast University, Nanjing, China
Introduction: This study presents a VT/VF algorithm using an adaptive variational
mode decomposition (VMD) method as well as a boosted classiﬁcation and regression
tree (boosted-CART) classiﬁer.
Database(s): ECG data from CUDB, MIT-BIH afdb and MIT-BIH vfdb is used.
This
respectively includes 35 records of 8-minute single-channel ECG data, 48 records of
30-minute single-channel ECG data and 22 records of 30-minute single-channel ECG
data.
Methods: The algorithm, inspired by Tripathy et al. (2016) [62], is initialized by pre-
processing of the ECG signals to remove baseline wander, power-line interference and
high-frequency noise. Each preprocessed signal is then decomposed into 5 band-limited
intrinsic modes (BLIMs) using an adaptive VMD method. This method aims to improve
the effect of the ECG decomposition by introducing a key parameter and a mode en-
ergy and independence metric parameter. Next, 6 features are extracted from the BLIMs.
Lastly, a boosted-CART classiﬁer embedding feature selection is used along with a 10-
fold cross-validation to distinguish between VT/VF and non-VT/VF. The process of the
algorithm is illustrated in Figure 4.4.

Mixed convolutional and long short-term memory network for the detection of lethal
ventricular arrhythmia
Authors: Artzai Picon1, Unai Irusta2, Aitor Álvarez-Gila1, Elisabete Aramendi2, Felipe
Alonzo-Atienza3,4, Carlos Figuera3,4, Unai Ayala5, Estibaliz Garrote1, Lars Wik6, Jo

1) Computer Vision Group, Tecnalia Research & Innovation, Derio, Spain & 2) Department of

Introduction: This study presents a deep learning algorithm combining a convolutional
neural network (CNN) with a long short-term memory (LSTM) network for detection of
VF.
Database(s): This study tests two data sets. The ﬁrst is out-of-hospital cardiac arrest
(OHCA) data from a previous study [15] while the second is formed using three public
databases, speciﬁcally 22 records from MIT-BIH vfdb, 35 records from CUDB and 10
records from AHADB, using only the ﬁrst channel from all three databases.
Methods: Initially, the ECG signals are preprocessed to remove low and high frequency
noise. Next, the ECGs are divided into non-overlapping segments, and certain types
of segments, found by consensus among two experienced biomedical engineers, are
discarded, leaving 96.3% of the AHADB, 82.6% of the MIT-BIH vfdb and 85.2% of
27
==PAGE:53==
4.3. CHOSEN TOP ARTICLES
State of the Art
the CUDB in the data set. The deep learning classiﬁer is then implemented, with an
architecture consisting of three elements. The ﬁrst element is a convolutional block with
two 1D-CNN stages, while the second element is a LSTM network. The output of the
LSTM network is fully connected to a classical neural network with one sigmoid output
layer outputting a probability, which, by use of a threshold, is used to perform the binary
classiﬁcation between shock and no-shock decision. The classiﬁcation performance is
evaluated by means of balanced accuracy (BAC), the unweighted mean of the sensitivity
and speciﬁcity.

Detection of Life-Threatening Arrhythmias Using Feature Selection and Support
Vector Machines
Authors: Felipe Alonso-Atienza1, Eduardo Morgado1, Lorena Fernández-Martínez1, Arcardi

1) Department of Signal Theory and Communications, Rey Juan Carlos University, Madrid, Spain & 2)
Arrhythmia Unit, University Hospital Virgen de la Arrixaca, Murcia, Spain
Introduction: This study presents an arrhythmia detection algorithm using a ﬁlter-type
feature selection procedure and SVM classiﬁers.
Database(s): ECG data from CUDB, MIT-BIH afdb and MIT-BIH vfdb is used.
This
respectively includes 35 records of 8-minute single-channel ECG data, 48 records of
30-minute single-channel ECG data and 22 records of 30-minute single-channel ECG
data.
Methods: The algorithm initially performs preprocessing of the ECG signals. The prepro-
cessed signals are used to extract 13 features, which are temporal, spectral and complex-
ity parameters. The temporal parameters include threshold crossing interval, threshold
28
==PAGE:54==
4.3. CHOSEN TOP ARTICLES
State of the Art
crossing sample count, standard exponential, modiﬁed exponential and mean absolute
value. The spectral parameters include VF ﬁlter, frequency content measures and me-
dian frequency while the complexity parameters include the complexity measurement,
phase space reconstruction, Hilbert transformation and sample entropy. The relevance of
these features is analyzed with different ﬁlter-type feature selection procedures. These in-
clude a correlation criterion, ﬁsher criterion and minimal redundancy maximal relevance
(mRMR) criterion. These feature selection procedures are combined with SVM classiﬁers
to build a high-performance classiﬁer. Two binary classiﬁcation problems are performed,
namely a shockable (VF plus VT) versus non-shockable arrhythmias problem and a VF
versus non-VF rhythms problem. The performance of the SVM classiﬁers is estimated us-

A novel algorithm for the prediction and detection of ventricular arrhythmia
Authors: Nourhan Bayasi1, Temesghen Tekeste1, Hani Saleh1, Ahsan H. Khandoker1, Baker
Mohammad1 and Mohammad Ismail1, 2019 [5]
1) Khalifa University of Science and Technology, Abu Dhabi, United Arab Emirates
Introduction: This study presents a machine learning algorithm for prediction and de-
tection of VT/VF episodes, utilizing 6 ECG features based on the variability of intervals
between ﬁducial points in the ECG.
30
==PAGE:56==
4.3. CHOSEN TOP ARTICLES
State of the Art
Database(s): 18 ECG recordings from MIT-BIH nsrdb comprise the normal group while
16 ECG recordings from the MIT-BIH afdb, AHADB and CUDB comprise the ventricular
arrhythmia group.
Methods: The proposed algorithm is initialized by preprocessing of the ECG signal, fol-
lowed by a detection and delineation of the P-QRS-T-waves. This is performed by a QRS-
complex detection using Pan-Tompkins algorithm [50], and then a T- and P-wave delin-
eation based on adaptive search windows along with adaptive thresholds, to distinguish
P- and T-waves from noise peaks, using the QRS-complex as a reference in each heart-
beat. The method is described further in Bayasi et al. (2014) [6]. In each of the 5-second
analysis windows used to perform the steps mentioned so far, 6 ECG-based features are
extracted and normalized. These are chosen based on evaluation of more than 50 features,
which are a mixture between previously proposed and new features. These include PQ,
QP, RT, TR, PS and SP intervals variability. A statistical signiﬁcance (p<0.001) between
the normal and ventricular arrhythmia group is seen for all 6 features. Lastly, a linear dis-
criminant analysis (LDA) classiﬁer is trained with different combinations of the features.
The process of the algorithm is illustrated in Figure 4.8.

Efﬁcient classiﬁcation of ventricular arrhythmias using feature selection and C4.5
classiﬁer
Authors: Monalisa Mohanty1, Santanu Sahoo1, Pradyut Biswal2 and Sukanta Sabut3, 2018 [42]
1) Department of Electronics Communication Engineering, SOA University, India & 2) Department of
Electronics & Telecommunication Engineering, International Institute of Information Technology,
Bhubaneswar, India & 3) Department of Electronics Engineering, Ramrao Adik Institute of Technology,
Navi Mumbai, India
31
==PAGE:57==
4.3. CHOSEN TOP ARTICLES
State of the Art
Introduction:
This study proposes an algorithm using time-frequency domain and
statistical features obtained from processed ECG signals. The classiﬁcation of the selected
features is performed using both a cubic SVM classiﬁer and C4.5 classiﬁer.
Database(s): ECG data from CUDB and MIT-BIH vfdb is used, which respectively
includes 35 records of 8-minute single-channel ECG data and 22 records of 30-minute
2-channel ECG data. However, several records consisting of only normal beats are not
considered in the feature extraction to maintain a balance between the number of VT, VF
and normal sinus rhythm (NSR) cases.
Methods: The algorithm initially performs preprocessing of the ECG signals to remove
baseline wander, high frequency noise and power line interference. From the prepro-
cessed signals, 13 features are extracted, which are temporal, spectral, and statistical pa-
rameters. These include a ﬁlter leakage measure, different spectral measures, band-pass
ﬁlter and auxiliary counts, covariance measure, frequency calculation, area calculation,
kurtosis, standard exponential algorithm, modiﬁed exponential algorithm and skewness.
Next, the feasibility of these features is measured via a Gain Ratio feature evaluator, which
ranks them according to their individual evaluation weightage. Lastly, a cubic SVM clas-
siﬁer with k-fold cross-validations and a C4.5 classiﬁer are used for classifying NSR, VF
and VT conditions. The process of the algorithm is illustrated in Figure 4.9.

domains, resulting in a good diversity of parameters. The C4.5 classiﬁer outperforms the
SVM classiﬁer with a 20-fold cross-validation, and this approach seems both innovative
and successful in classifying ventricular arrhythmias.
The articles described above all serve as inspiration for which approach could be most
beneﬁcial in developing an algorithm for detecting VT and VF, as is the goal of this project.
32
==PAGE:58==
4.3. CHOSEN TOP ARTICLES
State of the Art
Several other articles provide alternate algorithm suggestions as well as original versions
of the above-mentioned algorithms. These include:
• Fokkenrood et al. (2007) [16] present a VT/VF detection algorithm for a 24/7 per-
sonal wireless heart monitoring system, modiﬁed for smartphone use. An ampli-
tude distribution analysis (ADA) algorithm by Jekova et al. (2004) [32] is improved
by altering the order of the signal processing methods. This study places a beat de-
tector from Hamilton et al. [25] before a VT/VF classiﬁcation to obtain the heart rate
and beat type. Based on these, VT/VF events are detected and saved as 6 second
signals. Next, the presence of ﬁrst QRS-complexes, then dangerous heart activity
(DHA) and lastly asystole is investigated. If no QRS-complexes are identiﬁed, the
signal is ﬁltered and investigated for DHA. If no DHA is detected, the signal is in-
vestigated for asystole. Lastly, if the signal is not asystolic, a VT/VF classiﬁcation
is performed based on statistical distributions of QRS and VT/VF, computing three
amplitude ranges and comparing signal samples to these using thresholds, classify-
ing as either QRS or VT/VF. This results in an overall sensitivity of 97%, accuracy
of 98% and speciﬁcity of 98%.
• Tripathy et al. (2016) [62] propose an VT/VF detection algorithm based on a VMD
method. Using VMD, the preprocessed ECG signals are decomposed into a num-
ber of modes. Here, the energy, the renyi entropy and the permutation entropy of
the ﬁrst 3 modes are evaluated and these values are used as features. Next, the op-
timal features are then selected via a mutual information based scoring method.
Lastly, the classiﬁcation between shockable ventricular arrhythmia (VT/VF) and
non-shockable ventricular arrhythmia is performed using a random forest classi-
ﬁer. This results in an accuracy of 97.23%, sensitivity of 96.54% and speciﬁcity of
97.97%.
• Cheng et al. (2017) [11] present a VA detection algorithm based on personalized
correlation coefﬁcient features obtained from a patient-speciﬁc QRS-complex tem-
plate combined with various previously-existing features. The algorithm uses Pan-
Tompkins to detect QRS-complexes, and from these extracts six R-peak related fea-
tures followed by an average normal QRS-complex template, used to extract ﬁve
personalized correlation coefﬁcient related features. These are, in combination with
features from previous studies, input to an SVM classiﬁer. All possible feature com-
binations are tested, and the results in a speciﬁcity of 94.8% and accuracy of 94.66%
for the best two-feature combinations, as well as a speciﬁcity of 95.56% and accuracy
of 95.46% for the best three-feature combination.
• Ruiz et al. (2003) [57] present a VT/VF discrimination algorithm inspired by Chen
et al. (1987) [10]. The algorithm performs cross-correlation between two segments
of the same ECG signal, namely the ﬁrst 0.6 seconds of a 4.8 second windowed ECG
signal and the remaining 4.2 seconds of the same ECG signal. The irregularity of
the analyzed signal is then quantiﬁed using 3 parameters. Firstly, the number of
positive peaks of the cross-correlation function (Pn), where only those above 10%
of the reference signal energy are considered. Secondly, the standard deviation of
33
==PAGE:59==
4.4. REFERENCE ALGORITHM IMPLEMENTATION
State of the Art
the cardiac frequencies (stdf) for each window, and thirdly, the standard deviation
of the peak amplitudes (stda) for each window. Signal windows with Pn<8 are clas-
siﬁed as VF. For stdf and stda, the probability distribution functions are analyzed
for classiﬁcation, and these are used for development of thresholds used to classify.
The application of the algorithm to the given data results in a sensitivity of 90.20%
for VF and 96.75% for VT.
• Kim et al. (2014) [34] propose an extended version of the time-delay (TD) algorithm
by Amann et al. (2007) [3]. This extended time-delay (ETD) version is based on a
3D plot rather than the 2D plot previously used. While it still utilizes a 2D phase
space reconstruction (PSR), the number of overlapped boxes constitute the z-axis,
allowing for the inclusion of overlapped boxes, and overcoming the issue of sig-
nals with a changing baseline. This results in an improved performance compared
to TD, based on selected patients, of 4.05% in accuracy and 5.19% in speciﬁcation.
Speciﬁcally, the ETD algorithm achieves a sensitivity of 77.18%, accuracy of 83.91%
and speciﬁcity of 85.78%.
4.4
Reference Algorithm Implementation
In order to have comparability when developing the algorithm that will be the product of
this project, the VFPred algorithm proposed by Ibtehaz et al. (2019) [30] is implemented
as a reference algorithm.
The code is gathered in Appendix C, with the main script
visible in Appendix C.1. This is also to evaluate if the obtained results are reproducible.
For this purpose, supplementary materials are available including the code from Python.
Initially, data from CUDB and MIT-BIH vfdb are loaded into MATLAB, along with their
given annotations. This includes 35 records of 8-minute single-channel ECG data from
CUDB and 22 records of 30-minute single-channel ECG data from MIT-BIH vfdb. Each
signal is segmented into 5-second signals, by taking a segment of 5 seconds and then
sliding the window 1 second to take the next segment. In this process, the segments are
simultaneously labeled. Here, the annotations from the databases are used, respectively
rhythm annotations for MIT-BIH vfdb and beat annotations for CUDB. The segments
are labeled as VF if more than 50% of the samples in the segment have a VF label, and
non-VF if the opposite is true. For MIT-BIH vfdb, both the labels annotated as ’(VF’
and ’(VFIB’ are considered as VF while the remaining samples are labeled as non-VF. In
addition, MIT-BIH vfdb includes noise labels annotated as ’(NOISE’, and if the number
of samples in a segment labeled noise exceeds 33%, the segment is removed. For CUDB,
all samples between a ’[’ meaning "Start of ventricular ﬂutter/ﬁbrillation" annotation
and a ’]’ meaning "End of ventricular ﬂutter/ﬁbrillation" annotation are labeled as VF,
while the remaining samples are labeled as non-VF. In addition, if a segment in CUDB
contains any samples with the value NaN, the segment is removed. The segmentation is
veriﬁed by comparing to the number of segments given in the supplementary material.
Here, the total number of segments is identical, but the reproduced algorithm labels
approximately 850 more non-VF-segments than the VFPred algorithm.
34
==PAGE:60==
4.4. REFERENCE ALGORITHM IMPLEMENTATION
State of the Art
Next, the ECG-segments are preprocessed by subtracting the mean value from all the
samples. This is followed by a moving average ﬁlter of order 5, a high-pass Butterworth
ﬁlter of order 1 and cut-off frequency of 1 Hz and lastly, a low-pass Butterworth ﬁlter of
order 12 and cut-off frequency of 20 Hz.
The preprocessed ECG-segments are then entered into an EMD algorithm, computing
the IMFs and residue for each segment. This is followed by a computation of the noise
level and the noise level crossing ratio using a = 0.05. Based on these computations, the
relevant IMF components can be selected using b = 0.02. In addition, the DFT of the
segment, as well as the DFT of the IMF and the residue are computed. These are used to
calculate the cosine similarity. More speciﬁcally, the similarity measure of the DFT of the
signal and the DFT of the IMF as well as the similarity measure of the DFT of the signal
and the DFT of the residue is computed, which are to be used as features.
Next, the ECG-features are divided into VF and non-VF structures to create correspond-
ing label vectors. In this process, the labels are remade using the condition that 100%
of the samples in the segment have to be VF in order for the feature to be labeled as
VF and otherwise, the feature is labeled as non-VF. Subsequently, these are collected
to one feature matrix with an appertaining label vector, which are used in a random
forest ensemble with 750 decision trees. Hereby, the features are ordered based on their
importance and the top 24%, corresponding to 600 features, are selected and extracted.
Since the VF and non-VF structures are highly imbalanced, an upsampling technique
known as SMOTE developed by Chawla et al. [9] is used.
Lastly, the selected features are randomly shufﬂed and a 10-fold cross-validation is
performed. Inside this cross-validation, an SVM classiﬁer is trained on a training set
using an RBF kernel, a box constraint of C = 100 and a kernel scale
p
1/g with g = 45,
and a test set is used to test the trained model. The sensitivity, speciﬁcity, accuracy and
G-mean accuracy is calculated for each fold in the cross-validation. The mean of these
performance measures is also computed.
35
==PAGE:61==
5. VT/VF Algorithm
The goal of this thesis is to develop an algorithm able to detect VT and VF, henceforth
referred to as the VT/VF algorithm. To do this, the VFPred algorithm developed by Ibte-
haz et al. (2019) [30] is used as a starting point. VT is included in the model and various
improvements are made. As a reﬂection on the comments previously made in Chapter 4,
the data set is in this case divided into a train and test set, and the segments annotated as
noise are not removed, but rather added to the non-VT/VF-class. Initially, a description
of the included ECG data from public databases is provided. Next, a data segmenta-
tion is performed, followed by preprocessing, feature extraction, feature selection and
classiﬁcation. The performance is then attempted to be optimized and two models are ul-
timately obtained, one with an optimal performance and one with a performance optimal
for implementation in the WARD clinical support system. Lastly, the optimal model of
the VT/VF algorithm for the WARD system is veriﬁed using other public databases. The
code is primarily collected in Appendix D, with the main script visible in Appendix D.1.
Since the data from the WARD project has a sampling frequency of 100 Hz, and the used
public databases have a sampling frequency of 250 Hz, the same VT/VF algorithm with
a sampling frequency of 100 Hz is also created for comparison purposes.
5.1
Data Description
For the development of the VT/VF algorithm, the ECG signals from publicly available
databases, namely MIT-BIH vfdb and CUDB are used. An overview of these databases
is presented in Table 5.1, including the abbreviation used henceforth in this project, the
database name, the number of records in the database, the length of each of these records
in minutes, the sampling frequency used to digitize the database in Hz and the number
of available channels.

5.2
Data Segmentation
Each ECG signal obtained from MIT-BIH vfdb and CUDB is segmented into 5-second
signals, by taking a segment of 5 seconds and then sliding the window 1 second to take the
next segment. In this process, the segments are simultaneously labeled. Here, the rhythm
annotations from MIT-BIH vfdb are used while the rhythm as well as beat annotations for
CUDB are used. The segments are labeled as VT/VF if more than 50% of the samples in a
segment include ventricular ﬁbrillation, ventricular ﬂutter or ventricular tachycardia, and
non-VT/VF if the opposite is true. An overview of the amount and labeling of segments
for MIT-BIH vfdb can be seen in Table 5.2, while a corresponding overview for CUDB can
be seen in Table 5.3.

beat annotations ’VF’ and ’VT’ are included as VT/VF. These beat annotations are consid-

5.3
Preprocessing
In order to reduce the inﬂuence of noise, surroundings and circumstances on the ECG
signal, preprocessing is performed. A block diagram describing the procedure can be
seen in Figure 5.1 and includes a moving average ﬁlter, a high-pass ﬁlter and a low-pass
ﬁlter.

5.3.1
Moving Average Filter
The initial preprocessing step is a moving average ﬁlter. A moving average ﬁlter is a
low-pass ﬁnite impulse response (FIR) ﬁlter. By averaging a number of samples from the
input signal, single points in the output signal are created. The expression can be seen in
Equation 5.1.

In Equation 5.1, x is the input signal, y is the output signal while M corresponds to the
number of points in the average. [59]
The use of a moving average ﬁlter is optimal for reducing random white noise and still
maintaining sharp edges. This is illustrated on a rectangular pulse with random noise in

As seen in Figure 5.2, the number of points in the average, also known as the order
number, is a determining factor in the ﬁltering process. The noisy rectangular pulse from
Figure 5.2a is ﬁltered using a 10-point moving average ﬁlter in Figure 5.2b as well as a
50-point moving average ﬁlter in Figure 5.2c. Hereby, when the order number increases,
more noise is reduced, but the edges become less sharp. [59]
For the development of the VT/VF algorithm, the mean is initially subtracted from the
segment. Then, a moving average ﬁlter of order 5 is used. This should remove most of
the muscle noise while still keeping sharp edges. The appertaining code can be seen in
Appendix C.2.2.
5.3.2
High-pass Filter
To remove baseline drift, which can be caused by movement of the patient, DC-offset
as well as other low frequency noise, a high-pass ﬁlter is implemented. For the VT/VF
algorithm, speciﬁcally a Butterworth high-pass ﬁlter of order 1 and a cut-off frequency of
1 Hz is used and the appertaining code can be seen in Appendix C.2.2. By the concept of a
high-pass ﬁlter, this means that frequencies below 1 Hz are attenuated, while frequencies
above 1 Hz pass through the ﬁlter more or less without attenuation. To be speciﬁc, the
attenuation at the cut-off frequency corresponds to 3 dB, as a perfect ﬁlter is not feasible.

5.3.3
Low-pass Filter
Just as the baseline drift represents unwanted low frequency information, unwanted high
frequency information is also present and must be attenuated. For instance the power
line noise can become a factor if not reduced. To remove this, a Butterworth low-pass
ﬁlter of order 12 and a cut-off frequency of 20 Hz is used for the VT/VF algorithm
and the appertaining code can be seen in Appendix C.2.2. The concept of the low-pass
ﬁlter is to allow the low frequencies to pass without attenuation, while attenuating the
frequencies lying above a given cut-off frequency.

5.4
Feature Extraction
The purpose of the feature extraction is to transform the input data to a form from which
the model can ﬁnd patterns containing the biggest differences between the two classes
VT/VF and non-VT/VF. A block diagram describing the procedure can be seen in Fig-
ure 5.5. Here, an empirical mode decomposition, discrete Fourier transform and cosine
similarity measure is used.

5.4.1
Empirical Mode Decomposition
Initially, an empirical mode decomposition (EMD) is used as the ﬁrst step of the feature
extraction. EMD, developed by Huang et al. (1998) [28], decomposes a signal into a
number of intrinsic mode functions (IMFs) and possibly a residue as seen in Figure 5.6.
In this case, the signal is a 5-second preprocessed ECG-segment.

Figure 5.6: Example of using empirical mode decomposition on an ECG-segment. Inspired by

As seen in Figure 5.6, the oscillatory behavior gradually decreases as the IMF components
increase. Here, the ﬁrst and second IMF component, namely IMF1 and IMF2 contain the
majority of the oscillations from the ECG-segment. [30]
To elaborate, the local maximum as well as minimum is identiﬁed for a given signal and
used to obtain eu(t) and el(t), representing the upper and lower envelopes respectively.
These are created by connecting all the local maximum and minimum points via a cubic
spline. The mean of the upper and lower envelopes m10(t) is computed via Equation 5.6.
m10(t) = eu(t) + el(t)
2
(5.6)
To obtain the ﬁrst component h10(t), the mean envelope is subtracted from the original
signal as seen in Equation 5.7.
h10(t) = x(t) −m10(t)
(5.7)
If the IMF conditions are fulﬁlled, h10(t) would correspond to an IMF function. An IMF
is deﬁned as an oscillatory function with the number of extrema and the number of zero
crossings being equal or differing at most by one. Additionally, the mean of the local
maximum envelope and the local minimum envelope is zero at any point. However,
if these IMF characteristics are not yet fulﬁlled, h10(t) is used as the main signal and
the previous steps are repeated k-times until the IMF conditions are fulﬁlled as seen in
Equation 5.8.
43
==PAGE:69==
5.4. FEATURE EXTRACTION
VT/VF Algorithm
h1k(t) = h1(k−1)(t) −m1k(t)
(5.8)
Next, the IMF component is subtracted from the original signal to obtain x1(t) as seen in
Equation 5.9.
x1(t) = x(t) −IMF1(t)
(5.9)
Since x1(t) can include signiﬁcant oscillatory behavior, this is used as the original signal.
Equations 5.6 through 5.9 are repeated to obtain a number of IMF components until a
zero valued function or a small amount is left, also known as a monotonic function or
residue. [29]
For the development of the VT/VF algorithm, the EMD is performed on the preprocessed
ECG-segments, where the ﬁrst and second IMF components along with the residue are
considered. This decomposition can be seen in Equation 5.10.
x(n) = IMF1(n) + IMF2(n) + R(n)
(5.10)
In Equation 5.10, x(n) is the preprocessed ECG-segment, IMF1(n) is the ﬁrst IMF
component, IMF2(n) is the second IMF component while R(n) is the residue. [30]
Next, a noise level Vn is computed via Equation 5.11. More speciﬁcally, a percentage of
the maximum amplitude of the preprocessed ECG-segment is calculated.
Vn = a(max(x(n)))
(5.11)
In Equation 5.11, a is a constant, in this case set to 0.05, meaning 5% of the maximum
amplitude is calculated. Using Equation 5.11, the samples nL of the ﬁrst IMF component
that lie within the range −Vn to Vn are determined via Equation 5.12.
nL = t : t 2 n, |IMF1(t)| Vn
(5.12)
Then, a noise level crossing ratio (NLCR) is computed using Equation 5.13.
NLCR = Ân2nL IMF12(n)
Ân2nL x2(n)
(5.13)
Based on the NLCR from Equation 5.13, the appropriate IMF components are selected as
seen in Equation 5.14.
IMF =
(

if NLCR b
IMF1(n)
otherwise
(5.14)
In Equation 5.14, b is a constant, in this case set to 0.02. The appertaining residue from the
selected IMF components is also calculated by rewriting Equation 5.10 as seen in Equation
5.15.

Hereby, the selected IMF components and appertaining residue are used for further anal-

5.4.2
Discrete Fourier Transform
A Fourier transformation converts a signal from the time domain to the frequency do-
main. Certain interesting properties of a signal are often better expressed in the frequency
domain than in the time domain. For analog signals, a continuous Fourier transform
(CFT) deﬁned as Equation 5.16 can be used.
X(F) =
Z •
−• x(t) e−j2pFt dt
(5.16)
In Equation 5.16, X(F) represents CFT coefﬁcients, F is the frequency while x(t) is the
analog signal. [74]
However, for digital signals, a discrete Fourier transform (DFT) deﬁned as Equation 5.17
can be used. More speciﬁcally, a sequence of discrete points x(n) of length N is used as
the input signal.
X(k) =
N−1
Â
n=0
x(n) Wkn
N ,
k = 0, ..., N −1
(5.17)
In Equation 5.17, X(k) represent DFT coefﬁcients, k is a frequency index running from
0 to N −1 while WN corresponds to Equation 5.18, which can be rewritten via Euler’s
formula.
WN = e−j2p/N = cos
⇣2p
N
⌘
−j sin
⇣2p
N
⌘
(5.18)
Using Equation 5.17, the DFT coefﬁcients can be computed. Instead of evaluating the
coefﬁcients at any frequency as with an analog signal seen in Equation 5.16, these are
restricted to a set of frequency bins for a digital signal. The frequency intervals included
as frequency bins are determined by the amount of samples as well as the sampling
frequency in question. [74] [58]
For the development of the VT/VF algorithm, the DFTs of the ECG-segments as well as
the selected IMF components and appertaining residue are computed individually. These
are thereby resolved into frequency components and the code can be seen in Appendix
C.2.3.

5.5. FEATURE SELECTION
