5.4.3
Cosine Similarity Measure
A cosine similarity measure is used for the ﬁnal feature extraction step. This measure
estimates the similarity between two signals. More speciﬁcally, the cosine of the angle
between these two signals is calculated as seen in Equation 5.19.
similarity(A, B) = cos(q) =
|A · B|
||A|| · ||B||
(5.19)
In Equation 5.19, q is the angle while A and B represent the signals. [35]
For the development of the VT/VF algorithm, the cosine similarity measure between the
DFT of each preprocessed ECG-segment, and the DFT of respectively the selected IMF
components and the residue is computed, and used as the features henceforth in the algo-
rithm. Thereby, two expressions are used to calculate the features, namely the cosine sim-
ilarity of the DFT of the IMF referred to as similarity(xDFT, IMFDFT) seen in Equation 5.20
and the cosine similarity of the DFT of the residue referred to as similarity(xDFT, RDFT)
seen in Equation 5.21.
similarity(xDFT, IMFDFT) =
|xDFT| · |IMFDFT|
||xDFT|| · ||IMFDFT||
(5.20)
similarity(xDFT, RDFT) =
|xDFT| · |RDFT|
||xDFT|| · ||RDFT||
(5.21)
In Equations 5.20 and 5.21, xDFT is the DFT of the ECG-segment, IMFDFT is the DFT of the
IMF component while RDFT is the DFT of the residue. Speciﬁcally, the each cosine sim-
ilarity measure consists of the absolute value of each signal divided by a normalization
factor, consisting of the multiplication of the norm of each signal. This results in a feature
for each frequency, for both the IMF and the R. Therefore, the total amount of features is
twice the amount of samples in a segment. For example, for a 5-second segment with a
sampling frequency of 250 Hz, a total of 2 · (5 · 250) = 2500 features becomes the input to
the feature selection. The appertaining code can be seen in Appendix C.2.3.
5.5
Feature Selection
Feature selection is performed in order to decrease computational training time, as well
as to avoid overﬁtting of the model to the training data. A block diagram describing
the procedure can be seen in Figure 5.7 and includes data division into training and test
sets, a ReliefF algorithm used for feature selection and a synthetic minority over-sampling
technique used to upsample the data, and thereby handle class imbalance.
46
==PAGE:72==

5.5.1
Relabelling and Data Division
Before performing feature selection, the ECG-segments are relabeled as VT/VF and non-
VT/VF, based on the percentage of samples in the segment being annotated as VT/VF.
If 100% of the samples in a segment are annotated as VT/VF, the segment is sorted as
a VT/VF-segment, and otherwise the segment is sorted as a non-VT/VF-segment. An
overview of the amount of segments in each class is presented in Table 5.5.
Table 5.5: Overview of the amount of VT/VF- and non-VT/VF-segments after relabelling.
Class
No. of Segments
VT/VF
9638
non-VT/VF
52927
Next, the segments from Table 5.5 are divided into training and test set, with 80% of the
VT/VF-segments placed in the training set along with 80% of the non-VT/VF-segments.
The remaining 20% of the VT/VF-segments as well as the remaining 20% of the non-
VT/VF-segments are used as a test set. The corresponding label vectors are created, and
these segments are used for feature selection with the appertaining code available in Ap-
pendix D.2.2.
5.5.2
Relief Algorithms
For feature selection in the VT/VF algorithm, a ReliefF algorithm is used. This feature
selection method is included in the Relief family of algorithms. The basic Relief algorithm
is useful for two-class problems, while multiclass problems require the extended Relief
algorithm known as ReliefF. The basis of the Relief algorithms is to evaluate features
based on their ability to distinguish between observations that are close to each other.
This provides them with awareness of the contextual information, and allows for the
evaluation of feature quality even when the features are not independent, but possibly
share strong dependencies. [12]
The procedure of the basic Relief algorithm is as follows. Initially, all feature weights,
W, are set to zero. Then, a number of iterations, m is selected. For each iteration, m, a
random observation, I, is selected, and the nearest hit, H, i.e. observation belonging to
the same class as I, as well as the nearest miss, M, i.e. observation belonging to a different
class than I, are found. Finally, the weight is updated for each feature, F, individually,
using Equations 5.22 and 5.23. This procedure is repeated for each iteration, updating the
47
==PAGE:73==
5.5. FEATURE SELECTION
VT/VF Algorithm
weights accordingly.
W(F) = W(F) −diff(F, I, H)
m
+ diff(F, I, M)
m
(5.22)
diff(F, I1, I2) = |value(F, I1) −value(F, I2)|
max(F) −min(F)
(5.23)
The concept of Equation 5.22 is that if a feature displays very different values for F and
H, the diff from Equation 5.23 will be big, and thus the weight for that feature will be
reduced. Contrarily, if the feature displays different values for F and M, the diff from
Equation 5.23 will likewise be big, and thus the weight for the feature will be increased.
Thereby, higher weights means better features, and the best features can be chosen based
on the sorting of these weights. [12]
A number-based example is based on Table 5.6.
Table 5.6: Example observations for a Relief feature selection example.

is chosen as 2.

Next, an observation is chosen at random, in this case observation 2.
The nearest

nearest miss is found by calculating the distance to the observations in the other class.

followed by Feature 1 and lastly Feature 3. [12]
The basic Relief algorithm is limited to two classes, and unable to handle missing data.
Therefore, an extension is created, which is called ReliefF. This algorithm expands to a
k-nearest neighbours principle rather than 1 nearest hit and miss respectively, and uses
probabilities to handle missing data. The procedure is the same as for the Relief algo-
rithm, only instead of ﬁnding the 1 nearest hit, the k-nearest hits are found. Likewise
for the misses, which are found for every class C different from the randomly chosen
observation’s class. Additionally, Equation 5.22 is altered to Equation 5.24.
W(F) = W(F) −
Âk
j=1 diff(F, I, Hj)
m · k
+
Â
C6=class(I)
P(C)
1−P(class(I)) Âk
j=1 diff(F, I, Mj(C))
m · k
(5.24)
In Equation 5.24, the prior probability of the class, P, is used to weigh the contribution
for each class of misses. To include the class of the hits, the probability weight is divided
by 1 minus the prior probability for this class. [12]
For this VT/VF algorithm, a ReliefF feature selection algorithm with 5 nearest neighbours
is used. After the weights are computed, these are sorted in a descending order, and the
top 41% of the features are selected for further use. The appertaining code can be seen in
Appendix D.2.2.
5.5.3
Synthetic Minority Over-sampling Technique
The data set used for the VT/VF algorithm, and thereby both the training and test data
sets, is imbalanced, with many more non-VT/VF observations than VT/VF observations
as seen in Table 5.5. Speciﬁcally, the VT/VF-segments constitute 17.1% of the data set,
while the non-VT/VF-segments constitute the remaining 82.9%.
This is therefore a
moderate class imbalance. [72] To overcome this, a synthetic minority over-sampling
technique (SMOTE) is used to upsample the data. The VT/VF-class is upsampled until
there is an equal number of observations in each class.
What differs SMOTE from common oversampling is the fact that common oversampling
duplicates selected of the already existing samples, thereby providing no new input to
the model. SMOTE, in contrast, employs a k-nearest neighbour concept in order to cre-
49
==PAGE:75==
5.6. CLASSIFICATION
VT/VF Algorithm
ate synthetic data. In detail, a random sample in the minority class is selected, and its
k-nearest neighbours are found. New, synthetic samples are then created between the
minority sample and each nearest neighbour. It repeats until the minority class has the
same number of observations as the majority class. This procedure is illustrated in Figure
5.8. [72]
Figure 5.8: Illustration of SMOTE. The red circle represents a random sample in the minority
class, the black circles represent the k-nearest neighbours, while the green circles represent the
generated synthetic data samples. Inspired by [72].
This project utilizes a SMOTE function written by Bjarke Skogstad Larsen [37] and devel-
oped by Chawla et al. [9]. The appertaining code can be seen in Appendix C.2.4.1.

performance measures consisting of sensitivity, speciﬁcity, accuracy and positive predic-
tive value.

5.6.1
Support Vector Machine
To classify each ECG-segment as either VT/VF or non-VT/VF, a classiﬁer is needed. In
this case, a machine learning algorithm known as support vector machine (SVM) is used,
which more speciﬁcally is a supervised learning method. This method is applicable to
both regression and classiﬁcation problems, and is a commonly selected classiﬁer due
to its good performance coupled with relatively small computational power needs. In
addition, SVM is beneﬁcial for problems with a limited sample size, and will still work if
50
==PAGE:76==
5.6. CLASSIFICATION
VT/VF Algorithm
the number of features is higher than the number of observations. [18]
The concept of an SVM is to derive an optimal hyperplane in an N-dimensional space,
which separates the observations of two classes, by using the class labels provided for
each observation. Here, N corresponds to the number of features in the model. The
hyperplane will be created based on patterns of information found in the features. When
a hyperplane is found, this is then used to classify new, unseen data based on the same
features. The features are rarely the raw data, and more commonly speciﬁed during a
feature extraction process, in this case the EMD and DFT process. Two goals drive the
SVM, and need to be balanced. Firstly, the model wants to classify as many observations
correctly as possible when producing the hyperplane, which depends on the quality of
the used features, speciﬁcally how much relevant information about the wanted pattern
they contain. Secondly, the model wants to be general, so that it also works well on new,
unseen data. This depends on the number of unique observations used when training the
model, as this will widen the range of observations that the model can classify correctly.
[18] [52]
The optimal hyperplane will maximize the margin between the two classes. The expres-
sion for a linear hyperplane is given as in Equation 5.25.
wTx + b = 0
(5.25)
In Equation 5.25, w is an n-dimensional vector, x is the set of input training data and
b is a bias term. For this maximization, it uses support vectors, which consist of the
observations lying closest to the hyperplane, to create the margin which will then be
maximized when the maximum distance to each of the two classes is found. The margins
can be described using Equation 5.26.
wTx + b
(
≥1
for yi = 1
−1 for yi = −1
(5.26)
The distance between these margins are then maximized by maximizing the following
expression in Equation 5.27.
d(w, b; x) = |(wTx + b −1) −(wTx + b + 1)|
||w||
=
2
||w||
(5.27)
Maximizing the expression in Equation 5.27 is equivalent to minimizing w, which be-
comes a convex problem, as seen in Equation 5.28.
Minw,b = 1
2wTw
s.t.
yi(wTx + b) ≥1
(5.28)
In this case, where the two classes are completely linearly separable, it is called a linear
SVM with a hard margin, which is illustrated in Figure 5.10.
51
==PAGE:77==
5.6. CLASSIFICATION
VT/VF Algorithm
Figure 5.10: Illustration of a hyperplane, support vectors and appertaining margins of a linear
binary SVM classiﬁer with a hard margin in a 2-dimensional setting. [20]
However, using a hard margin makes the model very sensitive to outliers, as a low bias
and high variance is retained. To overcome this, the model needs to be allowed misclas-
siﬁcations, leading to a so-called soft margin. With a soft margin, certain observations
are allowed to be misclassiﬁed in order to ensure an increased performance on new data,
described as a higher bias and a lower variance. As commonly known, machine learn-
ing involves a bias/variance trade-off, meaning that a high bias is accompanied by a low
variance and vice versa. When allowing misclassiﬁcations, a penalty function needs to be
introduced. This can be seen in Equation 5.29.
F(x) =
N
Â
i=1
xi
(5.29)
In Equation 5.29, x is the distance between the misclassiﬁed data points and the margin of
their respective class. N corresponds to the number of dimensions. The linear SVM with
a soft margin is illustrated in 2 dimensions in Figure 5.11.
Figure 5.11: Illustration of a hyperplane, support vectors and appertaining margins of a linear
binary SVM classiﬁer with a soft margin in a 2-dimensional setting. [20]
Due to the introduction of this penalty term, the convex optimization function from Equa-
tion 5.28 is rewritten as Equation 5.30.
52
==PAGE:78==
5.6. CLASSIFICATION
VT/VF Algorithm
Minw,b = 1
2wTw + C
N
Â
n=1
xi
s.t.
yi(wTx + b) ≥1 −xi
(5.30)
Based on Equation 5.30, misclassiﬁed observations will be penalized. The parameter C
is equivalent to a box constraint, and is used as a trade-off between maximization of the
margin and minimization of the classiﬁcation error. [20] The larger the box constraint,
the larger the cost of misclassiﬁed observations, resulting in a more strict separation of
the data.
In a case where the classes are not linearly separable, the optimal hyperplane will present
with such a high variance that the model will not perform well on new data, as it is not
very generalized. To overcome this, a non-linear SVM classiﬁer can be utilized. In this
case, the input data, x, is mapped into a higher dimensional space, here called j(x), in
which a linear hyperplane can be used to separate the classes. Simultaneously, the data is
still non-linear in the input space. The mapping into a higher dimensional space to ﬁnd
an optimal hyperplane is illustrated in Figure 5.12.
Figure 5.12: Illustration of the mapping of 2-dimensional data in an input space into a higher
dimensional feature space, in which an optimal hyperplane can be found to separate the two classes.
This supports the concept of a binary non-linear SVM classiﬁer with a kernel function. [20]
This is the type of SVM classiﬁer used in this VT/VF algorithm. To map the observations
to a higher dimensional space, a kernel function is used, speciﬁcally a Gaussian radial
basis function (RBF) kernel. This is described in Equation 5.31.
K(xi, xj) = exp(−[||xi −xj||2]/2s2)
(5.31)
The Gaussian RBF kernel as seen in Equation 5.31 is a popular kernel function choice.
For this particular kernel function, an important property is that it is based on nearest
neighbours, meaning that observations close to a new observation will have a lot of
inﬂuence on how the observation is classiﬁed, while observations further away have less
inﬂuence. The amount of inﬂuence that the surrounding points have can be modiﬁed by
deﬁning a kernel scale. [20]
53
==PAGE:79==
5.6. CLASSIFICATION
VT/VF Algorithm
Transforming the input data to the higher dimensional space would be very computa-
tionally demanding, and this is avoided using the kernel trick. Basically, the kernel trick
involves computing the relationships between each point as if they were in the higher
dimension, rather than actually performing the transformation. To be speciﬁc, only the
inner product is needed, and can be found by selecting a kernel as seen in Equation 5.32.
j(xi, xj) = K(xi, xj)
(5.32)
As mentioned, in this case, the kernel K(xi, xj) is a Gaussian RBF as seen in Equation 5.31.
The kernel trick requires an expression for the vector w, as can be seen in Equation 5.33.
w =
N
Â
i=1
yiaij(xi)
(5.33)
In Equation 5.33, a is a Lagrange multiplier. The expression for a hyperplane can be
written as in Equation 5.34.
d(x) = wTj(x) + b
(5.34)
By substituting Equations 5.32 and 5.33 into Equation 5.34, Equation 5.35 is obtained, as a
general equation for the hyperplane of a non-linear SVM classiﬁer with a kernel function.
d(x) =
N
Â
i=1
yiaiK(x, xi) + b
(5.35)
As seen in Equation 5.35, it is no longer necessary to calculate the w vector to ﬁnd the
hyperplane, which concludes the kernel trick. This saves signiﬁcantly on computational
power. [20]
For the binary classiﬁcation problem presented here, namely with the two classes VT/VF
and non-VT/VF, a non-linear SVM with an RBF kernel is used. In addition, an optimal
kernel scale and box constraint are found by using cross-validation. Additional combina-
tions of kernel scales and box constraints prove useful for other algorithm requirements.
The SVM model is trained on the training set, corresponding to 80% of the data set, while
the performance is tested on the test set, corresponding to 20% of the full data set. The
appertaining code can be seen in Appendix D.2.2.
While the threshold for classiﬁcation normally classiﬁes by separating the classes at a pos-
terior probability of 50%, different classiﬁcation thresholds ranging from 0% to 100% are
explored, and the results are illustrated using a receiver operating characteristic (ROC)
curve, from which a desired model can be selected with sampling frequencies of respec-
tively 250 Hz and 100 Hz. An optimal model selection is performed using a Euclidean
distance measure. The Euclidean distance measure is in two dimensions deﬁned as Equa-
tion 5.36.
z =
q
(x2 −x1)2 + (y2 −y1)2
(5.36)

In Equation 5.36, (x1, y1) and (x2, y2) are coordinates for the two points, while z is the
Euclidean distance measure. [47] In addition, an optimal model for the intention of im-
plementation in the WARD clinical support system is chosen with sampling frequencies
of respectively 250 Hz and 100 Hz.
5.6.2
Performance Measures
To evaluate the performance of a binary classiﬁcation model, a confusion matrix as illus-
trated in Figure 5.13 is often used. In this case, the model contains the classes VT/VF and
non-VT/VF.
True Negative
False Positive
False Negative
True Positive
PREDICTED CLASS
TRUE CLASS
0
1
0
1
Figure 5.13: Confusion matrix of a binary classiﬁcation problem.
In Figure 5.13, the horizontal axis represents the class labels predicted by the developed
model, while the vertical axis represents the true class labels from the data. In this case,
the positive class will equal a VT/VF-class label as this is what the model attempts to
detect, while the negative class will equal a non-VT/VF-class label. True positives (TP)
refers to cases in which the model correctly predicts the positive class label, meaning
segments including VT/VF episodes are correctly identiﬁed as VT/VF episodes whereas
true negatives (TN) corresponds to cases in which the model correctly predicts the
negative class label, meaning segments not including VT/VF episodes, are correctly
classiﬁed as not including VT/VF episodes.
Likewise, false positives (FP) refers to
cases in which the model falsely predicts the positive class label, meaning segments
not including VT/VF episodes are incorrectly classiﬁed as including VT/VF episodes
whereas false negatives (FN) corresponds to cases in which the model falsely predicts
the negative class label, meaning segments including VT/VF episodes are incorrectly
classiﬁed as not including VT/VF episodes. [1]
Using TP, TN, FP and FN from Figure 5.13, several performance measures can be deter-
mined. Firstly, the sensitivity (Se) can be calculated via Equation 5.37.
Se =
TP
TP + FN
(5.37)
55
==PAGE:81==
5.7. PERFORMANCE OPTIMIZATION
VT/VF Algorithm
As seen in Equation 5.37, the sensitivity measure is computed by dividing the number
of correctly predicted positive cases, TP, by the total amount of actual positive cases, TP
and FN, in the data. Speciﬁcally, the percentage of segments including VT/VF episodes,
that are correctly identiﬁed as including VT/VF episodes is found. [1]
Next, the speciﬁcity (Sp) can be determined using Equation 5.38.
Sp =
TN
TN + FP
(5.38)
As seen in Equation 5.38, the speciﬁcity measure is calculated by dividing the number
of correctly predicted negative cases, TN, by the total amount of actual negative cases,
TN and FP, in the data. Speciﬁcally, the percentage of segments not including VT/VF
episodes, that are correctly identiﬁed as not including VT/VF episodes is computed. [1]
The accuracy (Ac) can be found via Equation 5.39.
Ac =
TP + TN
TP + FP + TN + FN
(5.39)
As seen in Equation 5.39, the accuracy measure shows the overall performance of the
developed model. The percentage of correctly predicted cases, namely TP and TN, of the
total number of available cases is computed. [1]
Lastly, the positive predictive value (PP) can be determined using Equation 5.40.
PP =
TP
TP + FP
(5.40)
As seen in Equation 5.40, the positive predictive value measure is computed by dividing
the number of correctly predicted positive cases, TP, by the amount of predicted positive
cases, TP and FP. The percentage of positive predictions, which are indeed positive cases
is found. [64]
To evaluate the performance of the VT/VF algorithm, the sensitivity, speciﬁcity, accuracy
as well as positive predictive value are used as performance measures. The code for a
function determining these can be seen in Appendix D.2.2.1.
5.7
Performance Optimization
After the initial development of the VT/VF algorithm, several attempts are made to im-
prove the performance of the algorithm, including cross-validating parameters and in-
clusion of other public databases. In addition, alternative methods for feature extraction,
feature selection as well as classiﬁcation are attempted.
56
==PAGE:82==
5.7. PERFORMANCE OPTIMIZATION
VT/VF Algorithm
5.7.1
Cross-validation
For optimizing the model parameters, cross-validation is used. To specify, parameters
such as the percentage of annotated VT/VF samples in an ECG-segment, which leads to a
VT/VF labeling of that segment, the percentage of top features to include after the ReliefF
algorithm, as well as the box constraint and kernel scale used in the SVM classiﬁer are
evaluated. Here, a range of values is chosen for a parameter, and a loop is created around
the model, where the model is trained and the performance is tested for every value of
the given parameter. Some parameters are cross-validated together, as for instance every
value of the box-constraint range is tested with every value of the kernel scale range.
The parameter, or combination of parameters, that provide the best performance is then
chosen for the ﬁnal version of the model.
5.7.2
Including Other Public Databases
In an attempt to include more variation and types of abnormal events in the VT/VF algo-
rithm, other databases are included for the training and test. Speciﬁcally, MIT-BIH svdb
[22] and MIT-BIH nstdb [46] are considered, and models are trained and tested while
adding each of these two databases individually to the originally used databases, namely
MIT-BIH vfdb and CUDB.
5.7.3
Feature Development
Several supplementary features are developed in the hope that these can improve the
performance of the VT/VF algorithm. The features are developed based on a posteriori
knowledge gained from visual inspection of misclassiﬁed ECG-segments, as well as ideas
arising from a priori knowledge about these signals and VT/VF characteristics. These
include envelope features, acceleration features and a sum of outliers feature.
5.7.3.1
Envelope Features
Initially, features based on the envelope of the signal are developed. The envelope of a
signal can be obtained via a Hilbert transformation, and the Hilbert envelope henv can be
determined using Equation 5.41. Both the upper and lower envelope can be calculated.
henv(t) = |sa(t)| =
q
s2(t) + S2
H(t)
(5.41)
In Equation 5.41, sa(t) is an analytic signal given as sa(t) = s(t) −iSH(t) while SH(t)
represents the Hilbert transform of a signal s(t). [55]
In this project, the upper envelope of the ECG-segment is initially extracted correspond-
ing to the rectiﬁed signal as seen in Figure 5.14a. The upper envelope is then divided into
ﬁve 1-second segments, which is illustrated in Figure 5.14b. For each second, the mean
of the envelope is calculated. These ﬁve mean values are gathered in a vector, which is
differentiated, leaving a vector with four values. The mean of this vector constitutes a
feature, while the maximum of this vector constitutes another feature. The full upper

envelope of the ECG-segment is also attempted as a feature. Lastly, the sum of the up-
per envelope is an attempted feature as well, corresponding to the area under the curve,

5.7.3.2
Acceleration Features
Features based on the acceleration are also created. To specify, the ECG-segment is differ-
entiated twice to obtain the acceleration of the curve, and the mean of this vector consti-
tutes a feature, the difference between the maximum and minimum of the vector consti-
tutes another feature, while the maximum of the vector constitutes a third feature.
5.7.3.3
Sum of Outliers Feature
A feature is created, which constitutes the amount of outliers in the given segment. An
outlier is in this case deﬁned as a point which is more than 9 standard deviations from the
mean of the signal, and the feature is created by summing the amount of outliers. This
results in one integer feature per segment.
5.7.4
Alternative Feature Selection Method
In the interest of improving the performance of the model, a random forest algorithm is
tested as a feature selection method.
58
==PAGE:84==
5.7. PERFORMANCE OPTIMIZATION
VT/VF Algorithm
5.7.4.1
Random Forest Algorithm
Since the most promising subset of features are identiﬁed through a random forest algo-
rithm in Ibtehaz et al. (2019) [30], this method is initially tested. A random forest classiﬁer
is an ensemble model, i.e. a combination of decision trees ultimately used to classify ob-
servations into classes. Decision trees are based upon asking a series of questions until
a reaching a certain conclusion, each question forming a new split of tree branches. At
every split, the decision tree will evaluate which question, or feature, that will split the
observations into groups that are as similar as possible within the group with as much dif-
ference as possible between the two groups. A simple example of this is shown in Figure
5.15. The decision tree will, if allowed, continue to make splits until a perfect separation
of the two classes is reached. [76]
Figure 5.15: Block diagram used as an example to illustrate a decision tree. Inspired by [76].
To get a random forest, several individual decision trees are combined. Each decision
tree casts a vote for an observation and the class with the most votes constitutes the
ﬁnal classiﬁcation.
This is based on the concept of wisdom of crowds, in that the
individual decision tree models have very low correlation, and will therefore collectively
outperform any one decision tree model. Using a multiple of trees will to a degree cancel
out the individual errors of the trees. However, a good performance of a random forest
classiﬁer is dependent on the presence of some sort of indications of the class or patterns
in the signal, as the performance otherwise will correspond to random guessing. [76]
To begin building the random forest classiﬁer, an ensemble aggregation method is
needed. Here, a very common method is called bootstrap aggregation or bagging. Using
the method bagging, a bootstrapped data set must initially be created. This means that
observations, or samples, are selected at random from the original data set, until the
bootstrapped data set is the same size as the original data set. The same observation
is allowed to be chosen more than once.
Next, a decision tree is created from the
bootstrapped data set, but using only a random subset of features for each tree. The
feature best suited to separate the classes at the particular node is then chosen, and the
remaining features are "passed on" to the next node, where a new random subset will be
59
==PAGE:85==
5.7. PERFORMANCE OPTIMIZATION
VT/VF Algorithm
found, from which the model can choose, and so on. This way a decision tree is created.
This process is repeated, with a new bootstrapped data set and new subsets of features
every time, creating as many trees as needed, and resulting in a large variation in the
trees. Then, as mentioned, each tree will cast a vote, and the aggregate of trees will make
a classiﬁcation. [60]
In this project, the random forest classiﬁer is used for feature selection rather than clas-
siﬁcation. In this case, a random forest ensemble with 750 decision trees and bagging
is created using all of the training data, which at this point is imbalanced. The optimal
number of included trees is found via cross-validation. Then, using a built-in MATLAB
function predictorImportance, the features that each observation contains are sorted after
importance. Then, the top 41% most important features are selected.
5.7.5
Alternative Classiﬁcation Methods
Other classiﬁcation methods are tested in the interest of improving the performance of
the model, namely a linear SVM model previously described in Section 5.6.1 and a naïve
Bayes model.
5.7.5.1
Naïve Bayes Model
A naïve Bayes model is tested as a classiﬁcation method. A Bayesian model can be de-
ﬁned as Equation 5.42 for a data set x and the appertaining class labels y, including C
classes and M features. This model is based on Bayes theorem, which describes the prob-
ability of an outcome occurring depending on a previous outcome occurring, known as a
conditional probability.
p(y|x1, x2, ..., xM) =
p(x1, x2, ..., xM|y) p(y)
ÂC
c=1 p(x1, x2, ..., xM|y = c) p(y = c)
(5.42)
If M becomes very large in Equation 5.42, the computation of the conditional probabilities
can be very expensive. Therefore, a naïve Bayes assumption is often used. It assumes the
conditional probabilities to be independent and thereby factorized as seen in Equation
5.43.
p(x1, x2, ..., xM|y) = p(x1|y) p(x2|y)...p(xM|y)
(5.43)
Inserting Equation 5.43 into Equation 5.42 results in Equation 5.44.
p(y|x1, x2, ..., xM) =
p(x1|y) ⇥... ⇥p(xM|y) p(y)
ÂC
c=1 p(x1|y = c) ⇥... ⇥p(xM|y = c) p(y = c)
(5.44)
Equation 5.44 is known as the naïve Bayes classiﬁcation model. [27] In this project, this
classiﬁer is used for the binary classiﬁcation problem between the classes VT/VF and
non-VT/VF.
60
==PAGE:86==

5.8. VERIFICATION
VT/VF Algorithm
5.8.2
Testing on Other Public Databases
To verify the quality of the optimal VT/VF algorithm for the WARD system, the databases
from Table 5.7 are respectively used as test data. Here, no annotations or labels are used.
To comply with the trained model, the test data from each database is resampled to re-
spectively 250 Hz and 100 Hz. Using the same procedure as conducted in the VT/VF
algorithm described in Sections 5.2-5.6, data segmentation is performed, followed by pre-
processing and feature extraction. The ECG-segments are then classiﬁed as either VT/VF
or non-VT/VF using the optimal VT/VF algorithm for the WARD system, and the to-
tal amount of each type of segment is calculated. The appertaining code can be seen in
Appendices D.3.2.1 and D.3.2.2 with the main script in Appendix D.3.1.
5.8.3
Veriﬁcation Procedure
After predictions are found for all segments in each database, the total number of VT/VF-
segments and non-VT/VF-segments for each database is calculated. In addition, these
are divided by their respective total number of segments, resulting in a percentage of
predicted VT/VF-segments for each database. This percentage can then be considered
with regards to the expected amount of VT/VF in the given database.
62
==PAGE:88==
6. VT/VF Algorithm for WARD System
The VT/VF algorithm developed in Chapter 5 is designed for use in the WARD clinical
support system. LifeTouch ECG data from a WARD arrhythmia study presents different
issues than the signals from the public databases, which the VT/VF algorithm is devel-
oped on. To adjust the algorithm to the WARD system, the existing algorithm is retrained
on the data from the WARD arrhythmia study and several improvements are made. Ini-
tially, a description of the included ECG data is provided. Next, a data alignment is per-
formed, followed by data segmentation, data processing including preprocessing, feature
extraction, feature selection and classiﬁcation. Lastly, the ﬁnal VT/VF algorithm for the
WARD system is veriﬁed by medical experts using WARD data. The code is primarily
collected in Appendix E, with the main script visible in Appendix E.2. Here, the code
necessary for implementation in the WARD system is ensured to be convertible to C++.
6.1
Data Description
For the development of the VT/VF algorithm for the WARD clinical support system,
LifeTouch ECG data from a WARD arrhythmia study is used. This data is collected using
the LifeTouch Blue ECG sensor from Isansys while a Holter monitor is simultaneously
collecting ECG data as well. The signals are recorded continuously with a sampling
frequency of 100 Hz for the ECG sensor and 128 Hz for the Holter monitor. Hereby,
the resolution of the signals from the ECG sensor is lower compared to the ones from
the Holter monitor. In addition, single-lead ECG is recorded on the ECG sensor while
the Holter monitor has multiple leads resulting in three appertaining ECG channels.
The Holter ECG data is automatically annotated by a program called Pathﬁnder SL
from Spacelabs Healthcare. These annotations should have been analyzed by cardiology
nurses from the cardiology ward at Rigshospitalet, but due to the current COVID-19
pandemic, it has not been possible to obtain the corrected annotations within the timeline
of this project.
The pandemic has likewise resulted in limited access to the WARD
arrhythmia study, where among other things, it has not been possible to access the wards
and see live demonstrations of the equipment being used, nor be involved in any setup
activities.
The clinical LifeTouch ECG data from the WARD arrhythmia study is collected from pa-
tients aged above 18 admitted to the cardiology ward at Rigshopitalet after a primary
percutaneous coronary intervention (PCI) procedure. The demographic including the pa-
tient ID, gender, height in centimeters, weight in kilograms, body mass index (BMI) as
well as presence of VT or NSVT episodes is presented in Table 6.1.

In Table 6.1, patient ID RAR009 is henceforth excluded due to the recording incorrectly
containing the data collected from a previous recording. In addition, patient ID RAR008
is also excluded, as the date of recording from the LifeTouch and Holter recordings are
different, and it is therefore not possible to create annotations that match the LifeTouch
recording. It is possible for the date of recording to be a mistake, as it is entered manually
for the Holter recording, and this has been the case for one of the other recordings, but
aligning the recording start times and thereby positioning the signals on top of each other
does not provide grounds for alignment, and the signals still do not seem to be from the
same recording, leading to the discarding.
6.2
Data Alignment
The given data includes annotations for the Holter recordings, but no annotations for the
LifeTouch data. Therefore, the Holter annotations are used as annotations for the Life-
Touch data after alignment of the two signals and thereby the annotations, to overcome
this issue. This alignment is performed manually for each patient, in a few steps described
here. Initially, the LifeTouch data is loaded, including the ECG signal and an appertain-
ing time vector. Next, the R-R intervals and appertaining time locations for the LifeTouch
data is loaded. Finally, the Holter ECG data is loaded, limited to the ﬁrst channel, and the
appertaining annotations are loaded as well and converted to a format which is readable
for MATLAB. Next, the Holter ECG data is resampled from 128 Hz to 100 Hz, which is
the frequency that the LifeTouch data is recorded at. Next, a time axis is created for the
Holter recording, and this is used to create the R-R intervals for this recording as well,
along with the annotation locations. The two R-R interval curves are plotted together as
a function of time, as can be seen in Figure 6.1a for patient ID RAR007. Then, two corre-
sponding points are identiﬁed on the two signals, and the difference is subtracted from
the LifeTouch signal, moving it on top of the Holter signal, aligning the two curves, as
can be seen in Figure 6.1b.
64
==PAGE:90==

After the alignment, the LifeTouch time axis is updated correspondingly. Next, the prob-
lem of time jumps in the LifeTouch recording is addressed. These are visualized with
an example of the LifeTouch ECG signal from patient ID RAR002, which can be seen in
Figure 6.3.
65
==PAGE:91==
6.3. DATA SEGMENTATION
VT/VF Algorithm for WARD
System
Figure 6.3: Visualization of the LifeTouch ECG signal from patient ID RAR002 in green with the
time jumps illustrated as red patches.
To overcome the issue of the time jumps visualized in Figure 6.3, the time axes are utilized,
and any samples present in the Holter recording but missing in the LifeTouch recording,
as illustrated by the red patches in Figure 6.3 are removed. Accordingly, any annotations
lying withing these removed samples are likewise removed. Next, the beginning and
end time locations of the LifeTouch recording are located in the Holter recording time
axis, and any LifeTouch samples occurring before the start of the Holter recording are
removed. Finally, the annotations and annotation locations are limited to those located
within the span of the LifeTouch recording, and the annotation locations are aligned to ﬁt
the new starting sample. The code for the data alignment can be seen in Appendix E.1.
6.3
Data Segmentation
Each ECG signal from the LifeTouch recordings in the WARD arrhythmia study is seg-
mented into 5-second signals, by taking a segment of 5 seconds and then sliding the win-
dow 1 second to take the next segment. In this process, the segments are simultaneously
labeled. This labeling is performed using the annotations from the Holter recordings,
which have been previously aligned to match the LifeTouch recording. These are beat
annotations, and the annotation will correspond to the R-peak of the beat. The labeling
using beat annotations is conducted identically to Section 5.2, but in order to make sure
that a full heart beat is annotated as VT/VF when such a one occurs, the VT/VF label
is started halfway between the current and previous annotation locations, rather than ex-
actly at the annotation location, enabling the inclusion of the P- and Q-waves in each beat.
Here, the segments are labeled as VT/VF if more than 50% of the samples in the segment
include premature ventricular contraction annotations and non-VT/VF if the opposite is
true. An overview of the amount and labeling of segments for the LifeTouch records in
the WARD arrhythmia study can be seen in Table 6.2.
66

6.4
Data Processing and Classiﬁcation
For the development of the VT/VF algorithm for the WARD clinical monitoring support
system, the same data processing procedure as in the VT/VF algorithm from Chapter 5 is
partially used. The workﬂow can be seen in Figure 6.4.
67
==PAGE:93==
6.4. DATA PROCESSING AND CLASSIFICATION
VT/VF Algorithm for WARD
System
ECG-
segments
Preprocessing
Feature
extraction
Feature
selection
SMOTE
SVM
classiﬁer
VT/VF
Non-
VT/VF
Figure 6.4: Block diagram describing the procedure conducted in the VT/VF algorithm for the
WARD system.
Initially, preprocessing of the ECG-segments is performed. This includes subtracting the
mean from each segment followed by a moving average ﬁlter of order 5, a high-pass
ﬁlter of order 1 with a cut-off frequency of 1 Hz and a low-pass ﬁlter of order 12 with a
cut-off frequency of 20 Hz. From the preprocessed ECG-segments, features are extracted
via an EMD, DFT and cosine similarity measure. In addition, the features developed
in the optimization process of the VT/VF algorithm on public databases are also used.
These include envelope features, acceleration features and a sum of outliers feature. The
code for the feature extraction can be seen in Appendix E.3.2.
Next, each segment is relabelled as VT/VF and non-VT/VF based on the percentage of
samples in the segment being annotated as VT/VF. If 90% or more of the samples in a seg-
ment are annotated as VT/VF, the segment is sorted as a VT/VF-segment, and otherwise
the segment is sorted as a non-VT/VF-segment. An overview of the amount of segments
in each class is presented in Table 6.4.
Table 6.4: Overview of the amount of VT/VF- and non-VT/VF-segments after relabelling.
Class
No. of Segments
VT/VF
1397
non-VT/VF
253574
The most promising subset of features are identiﬁed through a ReliefF algorithm. Here,
the top 41% highest weighted features are selected. A random forest algorithm is also
tested as the feature selection method. Once again, as seen in Table 6.4, more non-VT/VF
observations are available than VT/VF observations. Speciﬁcally, the VT/VF-segments
constitute 0.55% of the data set while the non-VT/VF-segments constitute the remain-
ing 99.45%. This is an extreme class imbalance and therefore, a SMOTE is used. [72]
Lastly, a binary classiﬁcation between VT/VF and non-VT/VF observations is performed
using a non-linear SVM with a RBF kernel. This classiﬁer is trained on the training set,
corresponding to 80% of the data set, while the performance is tested on the test set, cor-
responding to 20% of the full data set. The performance is evaluated by calculating the
sensitivity, speciﬁcity, accuracy and positive predictive value. Once again, classiﬁcation
thresholds ranging from 0% to 100% are explored, and the results are illustrated using a
ROC curve, from which an optimal model can be selected. The appertaining code can be
seen in Appendix E.3.3.

==PAGE:94==
6.5. VERIFICATION
VT/VF Algorithm for WARD
System
6.5
Veriﬁcation
To obtain a veriﬁcation of the performance of the ﬁnal VT/VF algorithm for the WARD
system, the trained model is tested on the WARD project database consisting of approx-
imately 700 patients. A random selection of 100 VT/VF-segments and 100 non-VT/VF-
segments are evaluated as either VT/VF or non-VT/VF by two medical experts, and their
classiﬁcations are compared to the predictions of the model in order to achieve a repre-
sentative measure of the performance of the algorithm.
6.5.1
Description of WARD Data for Veriﬁcation
The ECG data from the WARD project is collected using the LifeTouch Blue ECG sensor
from Isansys. The ECG signals are recorded for approximately 10 seconds per minute.
The clinical data is from post-surgery patients registered at respectively Rigshospitalet
and Rigshospitalet with Bispebjerg Hospital as well as COPD patients registered at re-
spectively Bispebjerg Hospital and Gentofte Hospital. The number of patients from each
group and appertaining hospital is presented in Table 6.5, resulting in a total of 677 pa-
tients.
Table 6.5: Overview of patient groups from the WARD project selected for veriﬁcation, including
the appertaining hospital and number of patients.
Patient Group
Hospital
No. of Patients

Rigshospitalet
430

Bispebjerg Hospital
45
COPD
Bispebjerg Hospital
188
Gentofte Hospital
14
6.5.2
Testing on WARD Data
Initially, the data is loaded from the WARD server and a data segmentation is then per-
formed, where the signals are divided into the appropriate recordings, each of approxi-
mately 10 seconds. This is done based on the appertaining time stamps, where a differ-
ence in time longer than 30 seconds between two samples is used as the cut-off to split
the signal into recordings. Next, the segments are preprocessed with the same procedure
as described in Chapter 5. The preprocessing is followed by feature extraction and clas-
siﬁcation, with the same procedure and model as developed in Section 6.4. Speciﬁcally,
each 10-second recording is given a prediction label based on the predictions belonging
to the 5-second segments contained in the 10-second recording. Two different methods
of labeling recordings are used. To elaborate, in the ﬁrst case, a majority vote principle
is used, meaning if a majority of the segments in a recording are classiﬁed as VT/VF, the
recording is given a prediction label of VT/VF, and otherwise, the label is non-VT/VF.
In the second case, a 100% VT/VF principle is used, where all segments in a recording
have to be classiﬁed as VT/VF for the recording to be given a prediction label of VT/VF,
69
==PAGE:95==
6.5. VERIFICATION
VT/VF Algorithm for WARD
System
otherwise the label is non-VT/VF. The 5-second segments are classiﬁed using the ﬁnal
VT/VF algorithm for the WARD system. The code is gathered in Appendix E.4, with the
main script visible in E.4.1.
6.5.3
Veriﬁcation Procedure
After predictions are found for segments in each patient group of the WARD data,
the total number of VT/VF-recordings and non-VT/VF-recordings is calculated for a
selected number of randomly chosen patients from each patient group. In addition, these
are divided by their respective total number of recordings, resulting in a percentage of
predicted VT/VF-recordings for each patient group.
For every patient, each 10-second signal is concatenated from the overlapping 5-seconds
segments. Then, the 10-second recording is classiﬁed as either VT/VF or non-VT/VF
based on the predictions mentioned previously in Section 6.5.2.
For approximately
50 subjects, if possible, two VT/VF 10-second signals and two non-VT/VF 10-second
signals are chosen, and the signals are saved. Hereby, a maximum of 100 signals of each
type is saved, and an appertaining label vector is created simultaneously. Afterwards,
the signals are shufﬂed randomly, and the label vector accordingly. The 10-second signals
are then plotted and saved as a pdf-ﬁle, with three plots on each page. Two pdf-ﬁles
are saved, one containing the plots with no labels and one containing the plots with the
appertaining labels. This is in the interest of having one ﬁle to send to classifying medical
experts, and one ﬁle to later verify the performance of the algorithm. The appertaining
code can be seen in Appendix E.4.2.6.
The created pdf-ﬁles without annotations are sent to two independent medical experts
from Rigshospitalet.
They are asked to classify each signal as either VT/VF or non-
VT/VF, and it is speciﬁed that they should be quite certain of a signal being entirely
VT/VF before classifying it as such. In total, they will be classifying 100 signals predicted
as VT/VF and 100 signals predicted as non-VT/VF. In case the two medical experts do
not agree, the pertinent signal is sent to a third medical expert, who will then act as a
tiebreaker. These annotations then constitute a gold standard to which the classiﬁcations
made by the algorithm are compared.
70
==PAGE:96==

7.2.3
Final Models
The results from the ﬁnal models of the VT/VF algorithm, namely one with an optimal
performance and one with a performance optimal for implementation in the WARD clin-
ical support system are presented.
7.2.3.1
Optimal Model
Using a Euclidean distance measure, the optimal model of the VT/VF algorithm corre-
sponds to Model 3 with a classiﬁcation threshold of 83% for a sampling frequency of 250
Hz and 84% for a sampling frequency of 100 Hz. The resulting performance can be seen
in Table 7.12.

7.2.4.3
Misclassiﬁcation Examples
Examples of ECG-segments before preprocessing, which are later misclassiﬁed by Model
3 of the VT/VF algorithm with a sampling frequency of 250 Hz are shown. Initially,
examples of false positive classiﬁed ECG-segments can be seen in Figures 7.9, 7.10 and
7.11.
Figure 7.9: Example of a misclassiﬁcation from Model 3 of the VT/VF algorithm. Four segments
are classiﬁed as VT/VF, but are in fact non-VT/VF, corresponding to a false positive (FP), while
the surrounding segments are correctly classiﬁed as non-VT/VF, corresponding to a true negative
(TN). The top plot shows the signal with dashed boxes indicating each segment, green for correctly
classiﬁed and red for misclassiﬁed. The bottom plots show each individual segment, green for
correctly classiﬁed and red for misclassiﬁed.

of the differentiated vector consisting of the ﬁve mean envelope values of each of the
ﬁve seconds, the mean of the acceleration vector as well as the number of outliers in a
segment. For the random forest feature selection, the same developed features are chosen,
except instead of the mean of the acceleration vector, the sum of the upper envelope is
selected.
