8. Discussion
8.1
Reference Algorithm Implementation
The VFPred algorithm proposed by Ibtehaz et al. (2019) [30] is implemented as a ref-
erence algorithm. The obtained results seen in Table 7.1 do not fully reach the VFPred
performance. Here, the achieved standard deviations are smaller than the ones obtained
in the VFPred algorithm, while a difference in performance of approximately 0.7% is seen
for all performance measures. The small difference in performance can be due to some
of the VF-segments being categorized as non-VF, as the SVM classiﬁer is thereby trained
to detect some VF-segments as non-VF or vice versa. In addition, converting the VFPred
algorithm from Python to MATLAB code can result in some differences. For example, the
SMOTE function is not a built-in function in MATLAB and therefore seems to operate dif-
ferently compared to the one from Python. This results in a need to over-sample synthetic
data instead of only real data. Nonetheless, when implementing a reference algorithm, a
decrease in performance from the original is quite common. However, the performance
can still somewhat be used as comparison and gold standard for the algorithm to be de-
veloped in this project.
8.2
VT/VF Algorithm
8.2.1
Development of the Algorithm
For the development of the VT/VF algorithm, initial changes from the reference algo-
rithm implementation are made, followed by a further development of the algorithm
using public databases.
8.2.1.1
Initial Changes from the Reference Algorithm
As previously mentioned in Chapter 4, a critical standpoint is taken regarding certain
procedures used in the VFPred algorithm. Firstly, Ibtehaz et al. (2019) [30] used the same
data set for training and validation, and the obtained results stem from respectively aver-
aging the performance measures from a hold-out cross-validation loop, without testing
on a separate test set. This is not considered ideal for representing the actual potential
and quality of an algorithm and therefore, in the algorithm development performed in
this project, the data set is divided into training and test. This step naturally leads to a
decrease in the performance of the algorithm, as the model is tested on data it has not
seen before. This is also a veriﬁcation of the robustness of the developed algorithm, as it
92
==PAGE:118==
8.2. VT/VF ALGORITHM
Discussion
in this case is trained and tested on different patients and every person displays a unique
ECG signal.
Secondly, Ibtehaz et al. (2019) [30] removed segments including more than 33% annotated
noise. This is an obvious way to increase the performance of an algorithm, as noise
decreases the signal quality and is something a model often has trouble classifying.
Removing the noise as such does not correspond to reality and illegitimizes the real-life
usability of the algorithm. However, if the noise had merely been included in the VFPred
algorithm, this would have been very valuable, but real incoming data does not have
annotations, and can therefore not be segregated prior to the signal processing.
To
preserve credibility, the algorithm developed in this project does not remove annotated
noise, but rather includes it as part of the non-VT/VF-class, allowing the model to learn
from it. This step naturally leads to a decrease in the performance of the algorithm.
Speciﬁcally, the performance of the VFPred algorithm when the different initiatives
mentioned so far, namely separating the data set and not removing noise, have been
corrected, is given in Table 7.2 as the top row, and is seen to have decreased prominently
from the initial performance presented in Table 7.1.
Additionally, the VFPred algorithm uses rhythm annotations from CUDB and beat
annotations from MIT-BIH vfdb, but beat annotations from CUDB are also available.
These are included in the algorithm development of this project, which results in a larger
amount of segments in the target class. Here, it is noted that it is odd for there to be a
difference between the beat and rhythm annotations in CUDB, as ultimately the same
episodes of VF should be present in the signal regardless of the annotation method. After
discussion with medical doctors, ventricular ﬂutter and ventricular tachycardia are also
included in the target class, joining ventricular ﬁbrillation. Thereby, compared to Ibtehaz
et al. (2019) [30], using beat annotations from CUDB as well as including ventricular
ﬂutter and ventricular tachycardia in the target class results in a slightly less prominent
class imbalance problem.
In fact, more than a doubling of target class segments is
obtained and assuming these initiatives are correct, this is a great improvement.
Since the goal of this project is to develop an algorithm able to detect both VT and VF, the
VFPred algorithm, which contains the classes VF and non-VF, is as mentioned expanded
to an algorithm with the classes VT/VF and non-VT/VF. However, it is not certain
whether adding VT to the target class is expected to increase or decrease the performance
of the algorithm. On one hand, VT has a morphology that is perhaps more similar to
a sinus rhythm than VF, making it more challenging for the algorithm when asked to
distinguish between a sinus rhythm and VT. On the other hand, a large amount of the
used features in this algorithm are frequency-based, and the frequency of VT is more
similar to VF than a sinus rhythm, which may be an advantage when distinguishing
between sinus rhythm and VT/VF.
When VT is included in this algorithm, non-sustained and sustained VT are not differ-
entiated. As mentioned previously, a non-sustained VT corresponds to three or more
consecutive ventricular extrasystoles terminating by itself within 30 seconds, whereas a
93
==PAGE:119==
8.2. VT/VF ALGORITHM
Discussion
sustained VT lasts more than 30 seconds. This categorization of VT is not speciﬁed, as
the concept of feature labels is used for the labelling of the segments. In this case, these
require 100% of the samples in a segment to be labeled as VT/VF, for the segment to be
labeled as VT/VF. This means that a VT/VF-segment will never merely include a single
ventricular extrasystole, but most likely more than three, and will in any case correspond
to a non-sustained or sustained VT. In consequence, some non-sustained VT episodes are
naturally missed by the VT/VF algorithm, but these do not persist and are harmless for
the patient, and this is therefore an acceptable premise of the algorithm. When VT in
this form is added to the VFPred algorithm after the initial changes along with the beat
annotations from CUDB, this results in a prominent increase in performance as seen in
Table 7.2 as the bottom row. The obtained performance is used as a starting point for the
development of the VT/VF algorithm on public databases, and various improvements
are made to achieve a higher performance.
8.2.1.2
Further Development on Public Databases
Initially, the VT/VF algorithm is designed on public databases with a sampling frequency
of 250 Hz, in order to ensure comparability with other articles and developed algorithms.
For preparation for implementation in the WARD clinical support system, the algorithm
is likewise developed with a sampling frequency of 100 Hz. Regarding the comparability,
a direct comparison can be drawn to the implemented reference algorithm by Ibtehaz et
al. (2019) [30], although it must be taken into account that this project chooses to compare
the developed algorithm to the VFPred algorithm after the initial changes instead of the
reference implementation, in order to uphold correct scientiﬁc standards, and compare
our algorithm to such.
The same public databases, namely CUDB and MIT-BIH vfdb are used for training and
test as in the training of the VFPred algorithm, although with the described altered way of
including the annotations, both for comparison purposes, but also because these are well-
chosen with regards to developing an algorithm focused on VT and VF, as both databases
contain both types of arrhythmias. In addition to VT and VF, both databases also include
other types of events and arrhythmias, such as atrial ﬁbrillation and other supraventric-
ular arrhythmias, as well as noise. This should make the VT/VF algorithm more robust
when confronted with real-life data, as other types of events and noise have been included
in the non-VT/VF-class, and it should therefore be better at distinguishing between these
and VT or VF. As touched upon previously, the algorithm will achieve better performance
measures if trained and tested without the presence of noise, as Ibtehaz et al. (2019) [30]
does, but this is not realistic and will most likely not result in an algorithm viable for
real-time use.
8.2.2
Optimization of the Algorithm
During the development of the VT/VF algorithm, several considerations are made about
how to optimize the initial performance described in Table 7.2. From the performed state
of the art literature study, 5-second segments are found to often be optimal and therefore,
94
==PAGE:120==
8.2. VT/VF ALGORITHM
Discussion
no changes to the length of the segments are made. The WARD clinical support system
also records 10 seconds of ECG per minute, creating a demand for short segments, that ﬁt
within this time frame, as the algorithm needs to run in real-time, and therefore leaves no
option for overlap between the 10-second recordings. Whenever possible with regards
to the time scope of the project, cross-validation is used to make decisions regarding
changes to the parameters of the VT/VF algorithm, as well as brainstorming and testing
different methods.
Initially, the most promising subset of features are identiﬁed through a random forest
algorithm, as this feature selection method is used in the VFPred algorithm.
In the
interest of improving the performance of the VT/VF algorithm, a cross-validation of the
number of trees in the random forest algorithm is performed without a noticeable change
in performance, and an alternative feature selection method is tested, namely a ReliefF
algorithm. When comparing the two feature selection methods in Table 7.15, the ReliefF
algorithm results in a higher performance for all tested classiﬁcation methods.
The
optimal combination is the ReliefF algorithm with the non-linear SVM classiﬁer, which
is the same classiﬁcation method used in the VFPred algorithm, followed by the naïve
Bayes classiﬁer and lastly the linear SVM classiﬁer. Since the alternative classiﬁcation
methods do not exceed the performance of the non-linear SVM model, this classiﬁcation
method combined with the ReliefF algorithm is used for the development of the VT/VF
algorithm. However, different hyperparameters than the ones from the VFPred algorithm
are used, which, as mentioned previously, are found via cross-validation. In addition, the
resulting performance of combining the random forest algorithm and ReliefF algorithm
respectively with the non-linear SVM classiﬁer is quite similar and therefore, the random
forest algorithm can be considered as an alternative feature selection method for the
development of the VT/VF algorithm for the WARD clinical support system, as the
computation time for the random forest algorithm is signiﬁcantly lower than for the
ReliefF algorithm.
During a previous point in the development process of the VT/VF algorithm, a cross-
validation over the percentage of included features has been performed while using the
random forest algorithm, which caused the change from the 24% used in the VFPred
algorithm to the 41% used in this algorithm. Unfortunately, due to the temporal scope
of this project, it has not been possible to redo this cross-validation with the ReliefF
algorithm, and this should be done in the future. For now, it is assumed that the same
factors that caused the 41% to be chosen at the earlier stage are still relevant, and would
result in a similar choice.
In an attempt to improve the performance of the VT/VF algorithm, new features are
created based on misclassiﬁcation plots such as the ones seen in Section 7.2.4.3, though
of course several more plots than the ones displayed are reviewed. For example, based
on the false positive cases in Figures 7.9, 7.10 and 7.11, envelope features are developed,
as a sudden change in the signal, which would provoke a sudden change in the envelope
of the signal, seems a common occurrence in these three cases. This is the reason for
developing envelope features based on a differentiated vector, so that the difference from
95
==PAGE:121==
8.2. VT/VF ALGORITHM
Discussion
second to second is taken into account, and the sudden changes thereby are captured.
An attempt was also made to include the full envelope, but this overload of features
overwhelmed the feature selection process and decreased the performance, so this was
not pursued. Based on the false negative case in Figure 7.13, acceleration features are
developed, as the presence of such a sudden spike is seen multiple times to disturb the
classiﬁcation process and cause misclassiﬁcations. Therefore, a feature that accounts for
the presence of these spikes in the training process might be beneﬁcial. From Figures
7.14 and 7.15, it seems that general noise, which obtains a slightly too steady frequency,
causes problems for the algorithm as well. It is hard to correct for this type of noise, but a
feature concerned with the sum of outliers in a segment is created to help the algorithm
deal with certain other types of noise.
However, as none of the developed features
are included by the following feature selection algorithm, they do not contribute to an
increase in performance for the VT/VF algorithm trained on public databases.
Another attempt at improving the VT/VF algorithm is to include data from other public
databases when training the model. Speciﬁcally, MIT-BIH svdb and MIT-BIH nstdb are
respectively added. The goal is to include more types of arrhythmias and other abnor-
mal events in order to make a more robust model. However, the performance does not
increase particularly with either database added, as seen in Table 7.14. Most prominently,
the performance decreases with the addition of MIT-BIH svdb, with a speciﬁcity of only
67.51%. In this situation, a very large amount of data is added to the non-VT/VF-class,
making the imbalance in the data set much larger than previously and the balancing done
using SMOTE becomes too extreme. While some performance measures seem better than
the optimal model presented in Table 7.12, the ultimate measure is in this case, due to
the trade-off between speciﬁcity and sensitivity, the accuracy, which is seen to be lower
for both models in Table 7.14, and therefore none of these databases are included in the
training and test data of the VT/VF algorithm henceforth.
8.2.3
Results of the Algorithm
Depending on the purpose of the given VT/VF algorithm, the difference in the perfor-
mances between Model 1, Model 2 and Model 3, as seen in Table 7.3 lies in the applied
hyperparameters in the SVM classiﬁer.
This is connected to the trade-off between
sensitivity and speciﬁcity, where the lower the sensitivity, the higher the speciﬁcity
becomes and vice versa.
Model 1 of the VT/VF algorithm uses a feature label percentage of 100%, a kernel scale
of 0.085 and a box constraint of 10. For a sampling frequency of 250 Hz, a sensitivity
of 72.95% and a speciﬁcity of 99.79% is achieved while with a sampling frequency of
100 Hz, a sensitivity of 72.93% and a speciﬁcity of 99.71% is obtained. As seen in the
confusion matrices in Figure 7.1, the very high speciﬁcity results in a very low number
of false positives. However, this is traded in for the lower sensitivity, which leads to a
larger amount of false negatives. In addition, the ROC curves in Figure 7.2 illustrate
the trade-off between speciﬁcity and sensitivity. Based on these, different classiﬁcation
thresholds, i.e. from 10% to 90%, are selected and the resulting speciﬁcity and sensitivity
96
==PAGE:122==
8.2. VT/VF ALGORITHM
Discussion
values seen in Table 7.4 show the trade-off possibilities. For a sampling frequency of
250 Hz, the displayed sensitivity ranges from 48.67% to 89.00% while the displayed
speciﬁcity ranges from 99.97% to 98.89%. These values are similar when using a sampling
frequency of 100 Hz, as seen in Table 7.5.
Model 2 of the VT/VF algorithm uses a feature label percentage of 100%, a kernel scale
of 0.105 and a box constraint of 10. For a sampling frequency of 250 Hz, a sensitivity of
81.93% and a speciﬁcity of 99.62% is achieved while with a sampling frequency of 100
Hz, a sensitivity of 81.98% and a speciﬁcity of 99.61% is obtained. Compared to Model
1, the same box constraint, but a slightly higher kernel scale is used, which results in
a noticeably higher sensitivity. However, this is once again traded in for a lower, but
still relatively high speciﬁcity. As seen in the confusion matrices in Figure 7.3, these
result in a noticeably lower, but still relatively large number of false negatives and a
slightly higher, but still very low amount of false positives. The ROC curves in Figure 7.4
again illustrate the trade-off between speciﬁcity and sensitivity. Based on these, different
classiﬁcation thresholds, i.e. from 10% to 90%, are selected and the resulting speciﬁcity
and sensitivity values in Table 7.6 again show the trade-off possibilities. For a sampling
frequency of 250 Hz, the displayed sensitivity ranges from 56.91% to 91.38% while the
displayed speciﬁcity ranges from 99.94% to 98.45%. These values are similar when using
a sampling frequency of 100 Hz, as seen in Table 7.7.
Model 3 of the VT/VF algorithm uses a feature label percentage of 100%, a kernel scale
of 0.68 and a box constraint of 30. For a sampling frequency of 250 Hz, a sensitivity
of 93.65% and a speciﬁcity of 99.16% is achieved while with a sampling frequency of
100 Hz, a sensitivity of 93.93% and a speciﬁcity of 98.89% is obtained. Compared to
Model 1 and Model 2, a higher box constraint as well as kernel scale is used, which
results in a much higher sensitivity and lower, but still relatively high speciﬁcity.
As seen in the confusion matrices in Figure 7.5, these result in a prominently lower
amount of false negatives and a slightly higher, but still quite low number of false
positives. The ROC curves in Figure 7.6 are in this case close to ideal. Based on these,
different classiﬁcation thresholds, i.e. from 10% to 90%, are selected and the resulting
speciﬁcity and sensitivity values in Table 7.8 show the trade-off possibilities.
For a
sampling frequency of 250 Hz, the displayed sensitivity ranges from 87.23% to 97.39%
while the displayed speciﬁcity ranges from 99.82% to 96.01%. These values are similar
when using a sampling frequency of 100 Hz, as seen in Table 7.10. Here, the smaller
classiﬁcation thresholds, i.e. between 1% and 9%, seem interesting and carry potential
for a desirable model for implementation in the WARD clinical support system, where
a high speciﬁcity is the biggest priority. The sensitivity resulting from exploring these
smaller classiﬁcation thresholds in Table 7.9 ranges from 31.52% to 86.26% while the
speciﬁcity ranges from 99.98% to 99.83% for a sampling frequency of 250 Hz. Once again,
these values are similar when using a sampling frequency of 100 Hz, as seen in Table 7.11.
From the ROC curves in Figures 7.2, 7.4 and 7.6, the optimal VT/VF algorithm found
using an Euclidean distance measure corresponds to Model 3 with a classiﬁcation
threshold of 83% for a sampling frequency of 250 Hz and 84% for a sampling frequency
97
==PAGE:123==
8.2. VT/VF ALGORITHM
Discussion
of 100 Hz.
This leads to a sensitivity of 96.73%, a speciﬁcity of 97.48%, an accuracy
of 97.10% and a positive predictive value of 97.46% for a sampling frequency of 250
Hz and a sensitivity of 96.46%, a speciﬁcity of 97.14%, an accuracy of 96.80% and a
positive predictive value of 97.12% for a sampling frequency of 100 Hz, as presented in
Table 7.12. The obtained performance is very good, but unfortunately not optimal for
implementation in the WARD clinical support system, as the speciﬁcity is not assumed
to be high enough to avoid system fatigue and numerous false alarms.
With guidance from medical doctors, an optimal VT/VF algorithm with regards to
implementation in the WARD clinical support system is selected. According to these
experts, as long as the speciﬁcity is very high, a sensitivity of approximately 70-80% is
sufﬁcient for the WARD system. Hereby, keeping the importance of a high speciﬁcity
while still preserving a relatively high sensitivity in mind, the optimal VT/VF algorithm
for the WARD system corresponds to Model 3 with a classiﬁcation threshold of 8%. This
leads to a sensitivity of 85.31%, a speciﬁcity of 99.84%, an accuracy of 92.58% and a
positive predictive value of 99.81% for a sampling frequency of 250 Hz and a sensitivity
of 83.69%, a speciﬁcity of 99.89%, an accuracy of 91.76% and a positive predictive value
of 99.86% for a sampling frequency of 100 Hz, as presented in Table 7.13. The resulting
performance is excellent for the intended use.
When comparing the confusion matrices from the optimal VT/VF algorithm and the op-
timal VT/VF algorithm for the WARD clinical support system, given in Figures 7.7 and
7.8 respectively, it is clear to see the prioritizations at work. While the optimal VT/VF
algorithm in total has fewer misclassiﬁcations than the optimal VT/VF algorithm for the
WARD system, they are quite evenly divided between the two classes. In contrast, the
optimal VT/VF algorithm for the WARD system is chosen with a small amount of false
alarms in mind, which shows in the very low amount of false positives, that come at the
expense of a higher amount of false negatives as well as a higher total amount of misclas-
siﬁcations. Overall, the performances obtained from both optimal models are higher than
the one from the starting point of the development of the VT/VF algorithm seen previ-
ously in Table 7.2. Hereby, the various improvements successfully increased the perfor-
mance. Furthermore, the performances do not differ much when changing the sampling
frequency from 250 Hz to 100 Hz as seen in Tables 7.12 and 7.13, which is positive for
the models. A slightly worse performance was expected for the 100 Hz model due to the
decrease in resolution, but this does not seem to pose much of a problem, which bodes
well for the implementation in the WARD system.
8.2.4
Considerations for WARD System Implementation
When choosing an optimal model for the WARD clinical support system, considerations
about the prioritizations of the performance measures must be made. Here, it is impor-
tant that the real-time alarms and the system in general do not lose their credibility with
the medical doctors and nurses, due to an excessive amount of false positives. This will
lead to so-called system fatigue, where the medical staff will to a decreasing degree take
the alarms seriously, undermining the beneﬁt of the WARD system, and reducing the
98
==PAGE:124==
8.2. VT/VF ALGORITHM
Discussion
life-saving possibilities that the reduced reaction time to ventricular arrhythmias could
otherwise provide. To avoid this situation, the aim is to have as few false alarms as
possible, where the developed algorithm detects a VT/VF episode when there actually
is none. Therefore, the speciﬁcity is prioritized over the sensitivity when it comes to
adjusting the algorithm and the subsequent performance. While high values of both
performance measures are of course desired, the sensitivity is allowed to decrease to
further a higher speciﬁcity, as a trade-off between the two measures is typically seen.
Furthermore, the uncommonness of VF in particular, but also to a degree VT, plays a
role in the prioritization between the performance measures. For the developed VT/VF
algorithm to be viable for implementation in a real clinical support system, a very high
speciﬁcity is needed. As the event occurs so rarely, many false alarms is a much bigger
risk, compared to if it was a more common occurrence. In the same way, the severity of
a VF event increases the demands for the algorithm, as the alarms become that much
more important. Missing a VT/VF event can have fatal consequences for the patient,
which leads to the considerations about the sensitivity of the algorithm. A low sensitivity
means that many of the actual VT/VF events are missed, which is traded in for the high
speciﬁcity. One could therefore argue that the sensitivity is more important due to the
fatal nature of the event, but it is important to remember that the LifeTouch ECG sensor
is not the only device on the patient, and that the developed algorithm is not the only
algorithm running on the server. Therefore, the consequences of a VF episode will be
caught by other physiological measurements, albeit slower than the algorithm would
allow the medical staff to react. This constitutes the real value of the VT/VF algorithm
in the WARD system, namely the time saved from occurrence to reaction, which can be
life-saving as well as crucial for the amount of complications that the surviving patient
will experience afterwards. Therefore, though the algorithm may not detect all VT/VF
events, even detecting some of them is of great value.
Testing various classiﬁcation thresholds enables us to develop a model, which can be
tweaked as wished. For the optimal VT/VF algorithm with regards to implementation
in the WARD clinical support system, the most desirable performances are found with
relatively low classiﬁcation thresholds. This indicates that the performance measures
are best when the model is asked to be very certain that a segment is VT/VF before
classifying it as such, while it does not need to be particularly certain of a segment
being non-VT/VF before classifying it as such. This coincides well with the strategy of
using feature labels for the labelling of the segments, where, in the case of the developed
algorithm, 100% of the samples in a segment needs to be annotated as VT/VF before the
segment is labelled as VT/VF. Therefore, it is logical that the model needs to be very
certain that a segment is VT/VF in order to only classify the segments containing 100%
of the VT/VF samples as VT/VF. For the optimal VT/VF algorithm, the classiﬁcation
threshold is found to be 83-84%, which is much higher.
This indicates that the best
performance measures are found when the model is not required to be particularly
certain of a segment being VT/VF before classifying it as such.
99
==PAGE:125==
8.2. VT/VF ALGORITHM
Discussion
This ability to tweak the performance to the priority for the given user is one of the
biggest assets of the VT/VF algorithm. As the priority in this project has been speciﬁcity
due to the intended use of this algorithm is for implementation in the WARD system, the
presented performances are mostly centered around high speciﬁcity values. However, it
should still be possible, due to the trade-off between speciﬁcity and sensitivity, to obtain
a very high sensitivity value as well through adjustment of the hyperparameters. For
this project, the ﬁnal speciﬁcity is considered a very successful performance, and viable
for implementation in the WARD system.
The obtained performance of the optimal VT/VF algorithm for the WARD clinical sup-
port system is, however, not perfect, and is worse than several other of the chosen top
articles. However, these selected articles relevant for comparison, namely the ones pre-
sented in Table 4.3, all have speciﬁcities below the one presented, with only one of the
algorithms reaching a speciﬁcity just above 99%. As previously mentioned, this is at the
expense of the sensitivity though, which then does not reach the level of these articles.
The performance is most likely affected by the noise present in the signals, which is com-
monly hard for classiﬁers to deal with. In addition, the fact that VT has been added to
the target class in this algorithm could cause confusion for the model, as VT as mentioned
previously can appear more similar to sinus rhythm than to VF. Finally, the large class im-
balance results in a need to upsample the VT/VF-class vigorously. As the SMOTE method
implementation in MATLAB has an upper upsampling factor limit, it is necessary to up-
sample in multiple iterations, meaning that already upsampled data is upsampled again.
The theory is then that the data points in the VT/VF-class will be so close that not enough
variation is obtained to balance the large variation contained in the non-VT/VF-class.
8.2.5
Considerations on the Performance of the Optimal Model
The optimal VT/VF algorithm is also not perfect, and has a performance that does not
reach the level of some of the algorithms presented in the chosen top articles in Table 4.3.
However, considering several of the approaches taken by Ibtehaz et al. (2019) [30], which
are considered incorrect or less than optimal by the authors of this thesis, it is plausible
that similar circumstances might exist in other of these articles. Even if this is not the case,
the performance of our algorithm is not far from the performances presented in Table
4.3, and even exceed certain ones. In particular, it is worth noticing that the algorithm
developed by Mohanty et al.
(2018) [42] has been developed using the same public
databases as this algorithm, providing grounds for comparability, and that our algorithm
can outperform theirs by slight adjustment of the classiﬁcation threshold, while the
obtained accuracy already exceeds theirs. Therefore, it is believed that our algorithm is
able to compete with the state of the art algorithms that have been investigated earlier in
this project.
Another consideration regarding the performance of the developed VT/VF algorithm
pertains to the annotations. The misclassiﬁcation plot in Figure 7.10 might induce a sus-
picious attitude towards the included annotations, as it does in fact seem plausible to
the authors of this thesis that the false positive segments could include VT/VF. The an-
100
==PAGE:126==
8.2. VT/VF ALGORITHM
Discussion
notations from the different public databases are of course widely used, and therefore
offers much in terms of comparison with other algorithms, but it is not possible for us
to otherwise judge the quality of the annotations or the qualiﬁcations of the annotators.
In addition, it has been previously questioned that the beat and rhythm annotations in
CUDB do not seem to be aligned. However, we have to assume that the annotations
are correct for the development of this algorithm, while keeping it in mind as a possible
source of decreased performance.
8.2.6
Veriﬁcation of the Algorithm
The veriﬁcation of the optimal VT/VF algorithm for the WARD clinical support system
consists of the testing on other public databases as seen in Tables 7.16 and 7.17, and the
robustness of the algorithm with regards to other types of events, as well as unknown
types of events and noise, is tested during this veriﬁcation process. The value lies in the
choice of databases to test on.
The ﬁrst database used as test data is MIT-BIH svdb. Since the included ECG signals are
supraventricular arrhythmias, the detected number of VT/VF episodes is expected to
be low. Testing on this database can investigate the skill of the developed algorithm in
distinguishing between supraventricular arrhythmias and ventricular arrhythmias. The
VT/VF algorithm ﬁnds 0.02-0.05% of the segments as VT/VF for the 100 Hz and 250 Hz
models respectively, meaning that the algorithm seems to be quite robust at handling
supraventricular arrhythmias.
The second database used for testing is MIT-BIH mitdb, which includes various types
of cardiac arrhythmias. The VT/VF algorithm classiﬁes 0.50-0.59% of the segments as
VT/VF for the 100 Hz and 250 Hz models respectively, and since approximately 0.31%
VT/VF-segments are included in this database, this is a good estimate of the performance
of the algorithm when faced with other types of events.
The third chosen database to test on, MIT-BIH nstdb, includes ECG signals originating
from MIT-BIH mitdb and is therefore assumed to include similar types and ratios of
events.
However, noise is added to these signals, and testing on this database can
provide insight to the developed VT/VF algorithm’s tolerance for noise. Considering the
classiﬁed percentage of VT/VF-segments, which is 0.07-0.11% for the 100 Hz and 250 Hz
models respectively, indicates a somewhat good handling of noise.
Finally, MIT-BIH nsrdb is used as test data. Since the included ECG signals are nor-
mal sinus rhythms and are therefore not supposed to contain any signiﬁcant cardiac
arrhythmias, the detected number of VT/VF episodes is expected to be very low. The
VT/VF algorithm ﬁnds 0.04-0.11% of the segments as VT/VF for the 100 Hz and 250
Hz models respectively, which is slightly higher than ideal, as long-time, continuous
monitoring is in mind for this algorithm, in which 0.11% does not seem as much, but
1662 false alarms in 1574886 segments, which corresponds to just over 18 days, is a
lot. Speciﬁcally, it corresponds to approximately 92 alarms per day, or 3-4 alarms an
101
==PAGE:127==
8.3. VT/VF ALGORITHM FOR WARD SYSTEM
Discussion
hour.
However, when resampling to 100 Hz, there is approximately 1-2 alarms an
hour, which is an improvement, although still not ideal. Testing on this database is a
very good indication of the quality of the algorithm for non-cardiac patients, as well
as an assessment of the magnitude of the risk of false alarms.
Ultimately, the algo-
rithm detects a very small percentage of VT/VF episodes, which is very positive, but the
amount of false alarms that occur might still be more than desired to avoid system fatigue.
Generally, the veriﬁcation shows that the optimal VT/VF algorithm for the WARD clinical
support system performs well when other types of events and noise are present, though
it is surprising that the 100 Hz algorithm performs better than the 250 Hz algorithm. This
might be due to some noise being lost when the resolution is lowered.
8.3
VT/VF Algorithm for WARD System
8.3.1
Optimization of the Algorithm
Initially, the same procedure as conducted in the VT/VF algorithm on public databases
is used for the VT/VF algorithm for the WARD clinical support system. However, this
results in a noticeably worse performance as seen in Table 7.18. Speciﬁcally, compared to
the optimal VT/VF algorithm, a decrease of at least 40% is achieved for all performance
measure values. Therefore, initial changes are made in attempt to improve the perfor-
mance, including testing an alternative feature selection method as well as changing the
feature label percentage. Since the obtained results from the optimal VT/VF algorithm
are quite similar when using a random forest algorithm instead of a ReliefF algorithm, a
random forest algorithm is attempted as a feature selection method. The random forest
algorithm also has a considerably more favorable computation time. The computation
of the ReliefF algorithm is very expensive and thereby results in limitations in terms
of cross-validation possibilities. The results of using the random forest algorithm can
likewise be seen in Table 7.18. Here, a slightly higher speciﬁcity, but a lower sensitivity,
accuracy and positive predictive value is achieved. Next, the feature label percentage is
changed, meaning instead of requiring 100% of the samples in a segment to be labeled as
VT/VF, for the segment to be labeled as VT/VF, a percentage of 90% is attempted. This
means an increase in the amount of VT/VF-segments, resulting in a slightly reduced,
but still extreme class imbalance problem. By using a feature label percentage of 90%
instead of 100%, the ReliefF algorithm results in a prominently higher sensitivity, but
a lower speciﬁcity, accuracy and positive predictive value while the random forest
algorithm leads to a higher sensitivity, accuracy and positive predictive value but a lower
speciﬁcity. Unfortunately, the different attempted approaches result in a minimal change
of the initial performance. The accuracy gives an idea of the overall performance of
the algorithm, and since the accuracies obtained via the two different feature selection
methods are quite similar, a decision between them cannot yet be made at this point.
However, the feature label percentage of 90% is chosen to be used henceforth, as more
VT/VF-segments are provided, thereby creating more variance and a slightly less
extreme class imbalance problem.
102
==PAGE:128==
8.3. VT/VF ALGORITHM FOR WARD SYSTEM
Discussion
In the interest of further improving the performance after the initial changes, the previ-
ously developed features, namely the envelope features, the acceleration features and a
sum of outliers feature are included in the VT/VF algorithm for the WARD clinical sup-
port system. In contrast to the VT/VF algorithm on public databases, three of them are
selected for the top 41% features after respectively the random forest and ReliefF feature
selection. Both methods chose the sum of outliers feature, which makes sense, as noise is
a more prominent issue in a real-time algorithm. However, one of the features chosen by
the random forest algorithm is not selected by the ReliefF algorithm and vice versa, which
can perhaps explain the noticeable difference in the resulting performance presented in
Table 7.19. Here, compared to the initial performance seen in Table 7.18, the random for-
est algorithm results in a much lower sensitivity, but a higher speciﬁcity, accuracy and
positive predictive value while the ReliefF algorithm leads to a higher performance in
all performance measure values. These are more evenly distributed and the trade-off is
not as prominent. The accuracy is also higher for the ReliefF algorithm compared to the
random forest algorithm, which again gives an idea of the overall performance of the
algorithm. Keeping these considerations in mind, the ReliefF algorithm is therefore cho-
sen as the optimal feature selection method for the ﬁnal VT/VF algorithm for the WARD
system.
8.3.2
Results of the Algorithm
The selected VT/VF algorithm for the WARD clinical support system uses a feature label
percentage of 90%, a ReliefF algorithm for feature selection, a kernel scale of 0.68 and a
box constraint of 30. With a sampling frequency of 100 Hz, a sensitivity of 80.73% and a
speciﬁcity of 73.30% is achieved as presented in Table 7.20. The resulting performance
is not optimal, but considering the initial performance obtained after using the same
procedure as the VT/VF algorithm on public databases, which performed very well, each
performance measure has increased with at least 20% meaning the various approaches
successfully improved the performance. The performance is illustrated via a confusion
matrix seen in Figure 7.16. Here, the low speciﬁcity results in a large amount of false
positives while the slightly higher, but still low sensitivity likewise leads to a high
number of false negatives.
In addition, the ROC curve seen in Figure 7.17 is not close to ideal, but still illustrates the
trade-off between speciﬁcity and sensitivity. Based on this, different classiﬁcation thresh-
olds are selected and the resulting speciﬁcity and sensitivity values can be seen in Table
7.21, showing the trade-off possibilities. Here, the spectrum is very broad, i.e. the sensi-
tivity ranges from 0.96% to 99.45% while the speciﬁcity ranges from 99.72% to 1.90%. The
classiﬁcation thresholds between 41% and 49% seem interesting and are explored further.
The resulting speciﬁcity and sensitivity values from these classiﬁcation thresholds can be
seen in Table 7.22. Here, the displayed sensitivity ranges from 58.08% to 77.97% while
the displayed speciﬁcity ranges from 83.33% to 74.79%. Keeping the importance of a high
speciﬁcity, but still a relatively high sensitivity in mind, choosing a ﬁnal VT/VF algorithm
for the WARD clinical support system is a difﬁcult task because of the non-ideal perfor-
103
==PAGE:129==
8.3. VT/VF ALGORITHM FOR WARD SYSTEM
Discussion
mance across the entire range of classiﬁcation thresholds. A classiﬁcation threshold of
43% is selected as the ﬁnal VT/VF algorithm for the WARD system, corresponding to a
sensitivity of 63.29%, a speciﬁcity of 81.49%, an accuracy of 72.39% and a positive pre-
dictive value of 77.37% as presented in Table 7.23. Once again, as seen in the confusion
matrix in Figure 7.18, the low speciﬁcity results in a large amount of false positives while
the slightly higher, but still low sensitivity leads a high number of false negatives. This
performance is not ideal and can be caused by many factors.
8.3.3
Considerations on the Performance of the Algorithm
The main factor of the decrease in performance of the ﬁnal VT/VF algorithm for the
WARD system is most likely simply the quality of the LifeTouch ECG data compared to
the ECG data from the public databases. The LifeTouch ECG sensor records single-lead
ECG, which leads to a decrease in signal quality and increase in noise, as well as the risk
of losing data if the connection is lost. Normally, this data could be substituted from one
of the other leads, but this is not possible with a single lead.
Secondly, the used public databases are tailored to ventricular arrhythmia use cases,
and therefore contain many more VT and/or VF episodes than the LifeTouch ECG data
from the WARD arrhythmia study. In general, the very small amount of VT/VF makes it
difﬁcult to properly train the model for several reasons. First of all, the class imbalance
necessitates a huge upsampling using the SMOTE strategy, and due to the small variance,
the relatively few variations of VT/VF that the algorithm is presented to does not create
robustness. In addition, ECG signals differ from subject to subject, and after alignment,
only signals from eight different patients are available, which once again limits the
variance of the VT/VF-class. Compared to the public database recordings, which are
around 8 to 30 minutes long, the continuous 24-hour recordings in the WARD arrhythmia
study presents new types of challenges, movements and noise. While this new type of
data recording decreases the performance of the algorithm, it also gives a more realistic
insight into the kind of data that will be available in the actual implementation of the
WARD clinical support system, and the new types of noise that will occur when the
patients suddenly have unlimited movement while the LifeTouch ECG sensor is still
recording.
Thirdly, it is not certain that the automatic annotations of the WARD arrhythmia study
data are particularly accurate. Unfortunately, due to the COVID-19 pandemic and con-
sequent busyness of the hospital staff, it was not possible for the cardiology nurses to
annotate the data within the time scope of this project, as was otherwise planned. A
good indication of the difference between the automatic annotations performed by the
program, and the ﬁnal annotations after the nurses have performed their analysis, could
be found if annotations for even one patient were available. As the nurses’ procedure
is to correct in the automatic annotations, it must mean that these annotations cannot be
trusted fully. Until then, it has to be assumed that the program is somewhat capable, but
this should also be considered a very likely contribution to the decrease in performance
of the model when trained on LifeTouch ECG data.
104
==PAGE:130==
8.3. VT/VF ALGORITHM FOR WARD SYSTEM
Discussion
8.3.4
Veriﬁcation of the Algorithm
The veriﬁcation of the ﬁnal VT/VF algorithm on the WARD system can be considered
as both the amount of VT/VF-segments found in the WARD patients as well as the
comparison of selected segments to a gold standard created by medical experts.
The confusion matrix seen in Figure 7.19 along with the results in Table 7.24 shows a
performance of the model on LifeTouch WARD data almost identical to random guessing
when comparing the predictions of the selected 200 segments to the corresponding
classiﬁcations performed by medical experts. While there is no doubt that the model
does not perform particularly well on the WARD data, it is in this case important to keep
the veriﬁcation setup process in mind. Firstly, 200 10-second recordings is not many and
had the time and staff capacity been higher, it would be preferred to include many more
signals in the veriﬁcation. Secondly, VT/VF episodes are such a rare occurrence that
selecting 100 signals of each type of prediction is not representative of the realistic distri-
bution. Not only are the chances of picking a false negative among the very large amount
of negative predictions very slim, but the same accounts for the true positives. Generally,
it is hard to ﬁnd any VT/VF episodes, if such ones even exist in the signals from the tested
subjects, and this disturbs the veriﬁcation process greatly. In this case, where many of the
performance measures lie around 50%, it is clear to see that these numbers arise from the
fact that the distribution of predictions chosen for the veriﬁcation are 50% non-VT/VF
and 50% VT/VF. If the speciﬁcity of the model had been much higher, then this veriﬁca-
tion format would prove more efﬁcient, and this was of course the hope for the model
when the veriﬁcation plan was created. Even in this case though, it would have been hard
to catch any false negatives without having medical experts verify every single recording.
As the intended beneﬁt of the veriﬁcation with medical experts has been partially lost
due to the very small amount of VT/VF episodes in these patients, the amount of
detected VT/VF-recordings in Tables 7.25 and 7.26 can be used as another indicator of
the quality of the model. Two different strategies with regards to classifying a 10-second
recording have been used. These include a majority vote principle, where the majority of
the segments in a recording have to be VT/VF for the segment to be classiﬁed as VT/VF,
the results from which can be seen in Table 7.25, as well as a 100% VT/VF principle,
where all of the segments in a recording have to be VT/VF for the segment to be classiﬁed
as VT/VF, the results from which can be seen in Table 7.26. As evident from Table 7.25,
the percentage of found VT/VF-segments is incredibly high when using the majority
vote principle, and therefore, the 100% VT/VF principle has been selected for further use
on the WARD data, as the percentages, as seen in Table 7.26, are signiﬁcantly lower. More
patients are available in the WARD database than included, but the time scope of the
project was a limiting factor, so a subset of patients representative of each hospital and
patient group was randomly selected. The results in Table 7.26 unfortunately show quite
high percentages of detected VT/VF, despite the knowledge that VT/VF episodes are
rare events, and it is therefore apparent that implementing this algorithm in the WARD
clinical support system as it is now would result in very many false alarms. However,
it was optimistic to expect the VT/VF algorithm to be fully ﬁnished and implementable
105
==PAGE:131==
8.4. FUTURE WORK
Discussion
within the narrow time frame that this project encompasses, and it should be considered
research in progress, to be continued further outside the scope of this project.
The misclassiﬁed signals shown in Figures 7.20 and 7.21 can be used as an indication of
what sort of challenges the ﬁnal VT/VF algorithm for the WARD system might have,
as well as be used to birth ideas for future improvements, such as development of new
features and so on. Only one false negative is available from this veriﬁcation, and it
is seen in Figure 7.21. However, 98 false positives are available from this veriﬁcation,
as given by Figure 7.19, and four examples of these are shown in Figure 7.20. Figure
7.20a shows the presence of higher frequency noise disturbing the signal, and how the
amplitude of the noise is quite high relative to the R-peaks in this case. Meanwhile,
Figures 7.20b and 7.20c show irregularities in the R-R intervals, which could be an
indication that the algorithm might beneﬁt from more frequency-based features, and this
could be kept in mind for future development. However, many misclassiﬁcations similar
to Figure 7.20d show themselves in the veriﬁcation, which is a testament to the current
quality of the algorithm, as there seems to be no indication of this signal containing a
VT/VF episode, but it rather appears as sinus rhythm.
As previously mentioned, the rare occurrence of VT and/or VF episodes creates a very
difﬁcult classiﬁcation problem, as has been experienced throughout this project in the
form of the very large class imbalances. Therefore, it needs to be realistically evaluated
whether it is a case where it makes sense to use signal processing to detect these events.
Even further, it should be considered whether it is at all realistic to create an algorithm
that can perform as desired in this classiﬁcation problem. Of course it would be a large
beneﬁt for the WARD clinical support system to be able to detect such severe conditions
as VT/VF episodes, but it is a matter of contemplating whether this detection is worth
the inevitable false alarms that will follow. Here, it is kept in mind that early detection
of VT/VF is extremely beneﬁcial for the patient, but that the WARD system includes
other wear-and-forget devices, that measure other physiological measurements, which
will eventually sound the real-time alarm as well in the event of a life-threatening VF. In
addition, the WARD system is so far meant for post-surgery patients in hospital wards
where cardiac death is not an overwhelmingly common occurrence, unlike for instance
on the cardiology ward.
8.4
Future Work
Before implementing the VT/VF algorithm in the WARD clinical support system, several
attempts to improve the algorithm can be made. The most obvious improvement attempt
is to train the algorithm on correctly annotated data analyzed by the cardiology nurses,
as was also the intention in this project. Subsequently, the development of additional
features might improve the performance of the model, especially if based on even more
misclassiﬁcation plots than already analyzed.
106
==PAGE:132==
8.4. FUTURE WORK
Discussion
While it was decided, in collaboration with the medical doctors, that making the target
class VT/VF was sufﬁcient, it could be beneﬁcial to distinguish between VT and VF
episodes in the future. For this purpose, we would suggest initially using an improved
version of the current VT/VF algorithm for the WARD system, and then developing an
additional, consequent algorithm, which also has a binary classiﬁcation problem, but this
time between VT and VF. This way, every time a recording is classiﬁed as VT/VF, the
second algorithm is used to ﬁnd out if this recording is VT or VF.
A big disadvantage of the current algorithm training procedure is the class imbalance
and lack of WARD subjects to train on. To slightly overcome this issue, it might be an
idea to train the algorithm on the public and WARD databases together, to increase
the amount of VT/VF-segments, while also generally adding variance to the data set
by including more different subjects. The WARD arrhythmia study could likewise be
expanded to include more patients and thereby increase the variance as well as the
likelihood of VT/VF episodes occurring in the data.
Another possibility to improve the performance of the VT/VF algorithm is to improve
the quality of the recorded WARD data, more speciﬁcally by for instance adding more
ECG leads to the measuring equipment. This could lead to more stable data acquisition
with less vulnerability to loose connections, and less susceptibility to noise. In addition,
the time scope of the project and the computational cost of the algorithm proved a big
limitation in cross-validating hyperparameters, feature selection methods as well as clas-
siﬁcation methods etc., and performing these cross-validations could most likely lead to
an increase in performance.
107
==PAGE:133==
9. Conclusion
In this project, a VT/VF detection algorithm is successfully produced with regards to
implementation in the WARD clinical support system after ﬁrst developing an algorithm
on public databases, and then training and adjusting the model for LifeTouch ECG data.
The ﬁnal algorithm consists of a data segmentation into overlapping 5-second segments,
followed by preprocessing, a feature extraction of an EMD, DFT and cosine similarity
measure, a ReliefF feature selection algorithm, a SMOTE upsampling to reduce class
imbalance, and lastly a classiﬁcation with a non-linear SVM and RBF kernel, evaluated
with relevant performance measures.
The optimal VT/VF algorithm on public databases achieves a sensitivity of 96.73%, a
speciﬁcity of 97.48%, an accuracy of 97.10% and a positive predictive value of 97.46% for
a sampling frequency of 250 Hz and a sensitivity of 96.46%, a speciﬁcity of 97.14%, an
accuracy of 96.80% and a positive predictive value of 97.12% for a sampling frequency of
100 Hz. This performance is very good, but unfortunately not ideal for implementation
in the WARD system, as the speciﬁcity is not assumed to be high enough to avoid system
fatigue and numerous false alarms.
Therefore, an optimal VT/VF algorithm for the
intention of implementation in the WARD system is selected, which achieves a sensitivity
of 85.31%, a speciﬁcity of 99.84%, an accuracy of 92.58% and a positive predictive value
of 99.81% for a sampling frequency of 250 Hz and a sensitivity of 83.69%, a speciﬁcity of
99.89%, an accuracy of 91.76% and a positive predictive value of 99.86% for a sampling
frequency of 100 Hz. This resulting performance is excellent for the intended use.
The ability to tweak the performance to the priority for the given user by use of classiﬁ-
cation thresholds is one of the biggest assets of the developed VT/VF algorithm. Since
the priority of this project has been to achieve a high speciﬁcity due to the intended
use of this algorithm, the presented performances are mostly centered around these
values.
However, it should still be possible, due to the trade-off between speciﬁcity
and sensitivity, to obtain a very high sensitivity value as well through adjustment of
the hyperparameters. The veriﬁcation on other public databases is a testament to the
good performance of the VT/VF algorithm, which, despite including supraventricular
arrhythmias, noise and other cardiac events, did not generate an excessive amount of
false alarms, emphasizing the high quality of the developed algorithm.
After training and testing on LifeTouch ECG data, the ﬁnal VT/VF algorithm for the
WARD system achieves a sensitivity of 63.29%, a speciﬁcity of 81.49%, an accuracy of
72.39% and a positive predictive value of 77.37%. This performance is not ideal and can
108
==PAGE:134==
Conclusion
be due to several factors, namely the quality of the LifeTouch ECG data compared to the
ECG data from public databases, the very small amount of VT/VF episodes causing a
need for a huge upsampling and most importantly, only having access to automatically
annotated data rather than annotations analyzed by cardiology nurses. The veriﬁcation
on WARD data conﬁrms the less than optimal performance of the developed algorithm,
which results in an overwhelming amount of false positive alarms at the current state.
However, the steps taken have been well considered, and represent a correct approach
in this scientiﬁc research context. Ultimately, it was optimistic to expect the algorithm
to be fully ﬁnished and implementable within the narrow time frame that this project
encompasses, and it should be considered research in progress, to be continued further
outside the scope of this project.
Future improvements of the VT/VF algorithm for the WARD system could include train-
ing and testing on annotations analyzed by cardiology nurses, development of additional
features based on misclassiﬁcation plots, training and testing on both WARD and public
databases to obtain more subjects as well as VT/VF-segments and thereby more variance
in the data set. Expanded cross-validation is also an option, along with an improvement
of the ECG recording equipment by for instance adding more leads. Ultimately, the rare
occurrence of VT and/or VF episodes creates a very difﬁcult classiﬁcation problem and
it should therefore be considered whether it is realistic to create an algorithm that can
perform as desired in this classiﬁcation problem, or if more common types of events
should be prioritized in the WARD system.
In conclusion, this thesis successfully fulﬁlled the project goals and presents a prelim-
inary VT/VF detection algorithm for implementation in the WARD clinical support
system. The optimal VT/VF algorithm trained on public databases competes with the
state of the art and is therefore described in an article draft seen in Appendix A, which
will be submitted for publication at the 43rd Annual International Conference of the IEEE
Engineering in Medicine and Biology Society. The real value of the VT/VF algorithm in
the WARD system is constituted by the time saved from occurrence to reaction, which
can be life-saving as well as crucial for the amount of complications that the surviving
patient will experience afterwards. Therefore, though the algorithm may not detect all
VT/VF events, even detecting some of them is of great value.

A. Draft for Article Publication
The following article draft is intended for submission to the 43rd Annual International
Conference of the IEEE Engineering in Medicine and Biology Society.
116
==PAGE:142==
CLASSIFICATION OF VENTRICULAR TACHYCARDIA AND VENTRICULAR
FIBRILLATION FOR A CLINICAL SUPPORT SYSTEM
Lena M. Nilsson & Maria E. L. Sakhat
Department of Health Technology, Technical University of Denmark
Abstract— Early warning scores are the current standard
for monitoring hospitalized patients. These are measured at
ﬁxed intervals up to 12 hours apart, possibly leading to fatal
events going unnoticed. The WARD project is a new clinical 24-
hour monitoring support system, for which this paper develops
a VT/VF algorithm on public databases, to detect otherwise
missed events such as VF and VT episodes. The algorithm
consists of a data segmentation into overlapping 5-second
segments, followed by preprocessing, a feature extraction of
an EMD, DFT and cosine similarity measure, a ReliefF feature
selection algorithm, a SMOTE to reduce class imbalance, and
lastly a classiﬁcation with a non-linear SVM, evaluated with
relevant performance measures. The optimal model achieves
a sensitivity of 96.73%, speciﬁcity of 97.48%, accuracy of
97.10% and positive predictive value of 97.46%, while the model
intended for WARD implementation achieves a sensitivity of
85.31%, speciﬁcity of 99.84%, accuracy of 92.58% and positive
predictive value of 99.81%. The developed model is believed to
be able to compete with the state of the art, though the rare
occurrence of VT and/or VF episodes creates a very difﬁcult
classiﬁcation problem.
I. INTRODUCTION
Cardiovascular disease is the leading cause of death world-
wide. In 2016, approximately 17.9 million [10] people died
from cardiovascular diseases, corresponding to 31% [10]
of all global deaths. Many of these cardiovascular diseases
can evolve into cardiac arrhythmias and subsequently death.
Currently, hospitalized patients are monitored using early
warning scores at ﬁxed intervals up to 12 hours apart,
possibly leading to fatal events going unnoticed, which
results in a slower reaction time for the medical staff. The
Wireless Assessment of Respiratory and circulatory Distress
(WARD) project is a new clinical 24-hour monitoring support
system using small non-intrusive wireless devices coupled
with intelligent algorithms. Hereby, the medical staff can
continuously monitor patients and detect otherwise missed
events, for instance a ventricular ﬁbrillation (VF) episode. VF
commonly follows ventricular tachycardia (VT), precedes
sudden cardiac death, and is present in 70% [7] of cardiac
arrest patients. Therefore, a VT/VF detection algorithm is
developed on public databases and adjusted with regards to
implementation in the WARD system.
II. DATA DESCRIPTION
ECG signals from publicly available databases, namely
MIT-BIH vfdb [3] and CUDB [8] are used. An overview
of these databases is presented in Table I, including the
abbreviation used henceforth, the database name, the number
of records in the database, the record lengths in minutes, the
sampling frequency used to digitize the database in Hz and
the number of available channels. Speciﬁcally, 22 records of
30-minute ECG data from MIT-BIH vfdb and 35 records of
8-minute ECG data from CUDB are used, the ﬁrst channel
from each database.
III. METHODS
Using the VFPred algorithm proposed by Ibtehaz et al.
(2019) [5] as a reference algorithm and starting point, a
block diagram describing the procedure of development of
the VT/VF algorithm can be seen in Figure 1.
A. Data Segmentation
Each ECG signal from MIT-BIH vfdb and CUDB is
segmented into 5-second signals, by taking a segment of
5 seconds and then sliding the window 1 second to take
the next segment, while the segments are simultaneously
labeled. Here, the rhythm annotations from MIT-BIH vfdb
are used while the rhythm as well as beat annotations for
CUDB are used. The annotations are used for labeling at
a later stage. More speciﬁcally, the beat annotations ’(VF’,
’(VFIB’, ’(VFL’ and ’(VT’ for MIT-BIH vfdb and ’VF’ and
’VT’ for CUDB are included as VT/VF annotations. These
beat annotations are considered as the beginning of a VT/VF
episode while any remaining types of beat annotations are
considered as the beginning of a non-VT/VF episode. For
each beat annotation, all samples in between that annotation
and the following annotation are then given that label. For the
rhythm annotations in CUDB, all the samples between a ’[’
meaning ”Start of ventricular ﬂutter/ﬁbrillation” annotation
and a ’]’ meaning ”End of ventricular ﬂutter/ﬁbrillation”
annotation are also labeled as VT/VF. In addition, CUDB
includes samples with the value NaN and segments including
any of these are removed, a total of 1165 segments.
B. Preprocessing
Preprocessing of the ECG-segments is performed and
includes subtracting the mean from each segment followed
by a moving average ﬁlter of order 5, a high-pass ﬁlter of
order 1 with a cut-off frequency of 1 Hz and a low-pass ﬁlter
of order 12 with a cut-off frequency of 20 Hz.
C. Feature Extraction
Empirical mode decomposition (EMD), developed by
Huang et al. (1998) [4], decomposes a signal into a number
of intrinsic mode functions (IMFs) and possibly a residue.
The oscillatory behavior gradually decreases as the IMF

Block diagram describing the procedure conducted for the development of the VT/VF algorithm.
components increase, and the ﬁrst and second IMF com-
ponent, namely IMF1 and IMF2, will contain the majority
of the oscillations from the ECG-segment. To elaborate,
the local maximum and minimum is identiﬁed for a given
signal and used to obtain the upper and lower envelopes
respectively. These are created by connecting all the local
maximum and minimum points via a cubic spline. The
decomposition can be seen in Equation 1.
x(n) = IMF1(n) + IMF2(n) + R(n)
(1)
In Equation 1, x(n) is the preprocessed ECG-segment,
IMF1(n) is the ﬁrst IMF component, IMF2(n) is the second
IMF component while R(n) is the residue. [5] Next, a noise
level Vn is computed via Equation 2 as a percentage of the
maximum amplitude of the preprocessed ECG-segment.
Vn = ↵(max(x(n)))
(2)
In Equation 2, ↵is a constant, in this case set to 0.05.
Using Equation 2, the samples, nL, of IMF1 that lie within
the range −Vn to Vn are determined via Equation 3.
nL = t : t 2 n, |IMF1(t)| Vn
(3)
Then, a noise level crossing ratio (NLCR) is computed
using Equation 4.
NLCR =
P
n2nL IMF1
2(n)
P
n2nL x2(n)
(4)
Based on the NLCR from Equation 4, the appropriate IMF
components are selected as seen in Equation 5.
IMF =
(
IMF1(n) + IMF2(n)
if NLCR β
IMF1(n)
otherwise
(5)
In Equation 5, β is a constant, in this case set to 0.02.
The appertaining residue from the selected IMF components
is calculated by rewriting Equation 1 as seen in Equation 6.
R(n) = x(n) −IMF1(n) −IMF2(n)
(6)
Hereby, the selected IMF components and appertaining
residue are found by Equation 6. [5]
Furthermore, the discrete Fourier transforms (DFTs) of the
ECG-segments as well as the selected IMF components and
appertaining residue are computed individually. For the ﬁnal
feature conversion, a cosine similarity measure is used. More
speciﬁcally, the cosine of the angle between these two signals
is calculated as seen in Equation 7.
similarity(A, B) = cos(✓) =
|A · B|
||A|| · ||B||
(7)
In Equation 7, ✓is the angle while A and B represent the
signals. [6] For the development of the VT/VF algorithm,
the cosine similarity measure between the DFT of each
preprocessed ECG-segment and the DFT of respectively the
selected IMF components and the residue is computed, and
used as the features henceforth in the algorithm.
D. Feature Selection
Before performing feature selection, each ECG-segment is
labelled as VT/VF or non-VT/VF based on the percentage of
samples in the segment annotated as VT/VF. If 100% of the
samples in a segment are annotated as VT/VF, the segment is
sorted as a VT/VF-segment, and otherwise as a non-VT/VF-
segment. An overview of the amount of segments in each
class is presented in Table II.
TABLE II
OVERVIEW OF AMOUNT OF VT/VF- AND NON-VT/VF-SEGMENTS.
Class
No. of Segments
VT/VF
9648
non-VT/VF
52917
The segments from Table II are divided into training and
test sets, with 80% of the VT/VF-segments placed in the
training set along with 80% of the non-VT/VF-segments.
The remaining 20% of the VT/VF- as well as non-VT/VF-
segments are respectively used as a test set. Appertaining
label vectors are created as well. These segments are used
in a ReliefF feature selection algorithm.
The basic Relief algorithm is useful for two-class prob-
lems, while multiclass problems require the extended Re-
lief algorithm known as ReliefF. The basis of the Relief
algorithms is to evaluate features based on their ability
==PAGE:144==
to distinguish between observations that are close to each
other. This provides them with awareness of the contextual
information, and allows for the evaluation of feature quality
even when the features are not independent, but possibly
share strong dependencies. While the basic Relief algorithm
is limited to two classes, and unable to handle missing data,
ReliefF expands to a k-nearest neighbours principle and uses
probabilities to handle missing data. Equation 8 is used to
update the weights, W, for each feature, F, based on a
randomly selected observation, I.
W(F) = W(F) −
Pk
j=1 di↵(F,I,Hj)
m·k
+
P
C6=class(I)
P (C)
1−P (class(I))
Pk
j=1 di↵(F,I,Mj(C))
m·k
(8)
In Equation 8, the prior probability of the class, P, is
used to weigh the contribution for each class, C, of misses,
M. To include the class of the hits, H, the probability
weight is divided by 1 minus the prior probability for this
class. [1] In this case, the ReliefF algorithm is used with 5
nearest neighbours. After the weights are computed, these
are sorted in a descending order, and the top 41% of the
features are selected for further use.
As seen in Table II, the data set is imbalanced, with
many more non-VT/VF observations than VT/VF observa-
tions. Speciﬁcally, the VT/VF-segments constitute 17.1%
of the data set, while the non-VT/VF-segments constitute
the remaining 82.9%. This is therefore a moderate class
imbalance. To overcome this, a synthetic minority over-
sampling technique (SMOTE) is used to upsample the data.
This method employs a k-nearest neighbour concept in order
to create synthetic data, and the VT/VF-class is upsampled
until there is an equal number of observations in each class.
E. Classiﬁcation
To classify each ECG-segment as either VT/VF or
non-VT/VF, a support vector machine (SVM) is used,
which is a supervised learning method applicable to both
regression and classiﬁcation problems, and a commonly
selected classiﬁer due to its good performance and relatively
small computational cost. The concept of an SVM is to
derive an optimal hyperplane in an N-dimensional space,
which separates the observations of two classes by using
the class labels provided for each observation. Here, N
corresponds to the number of features in the model. The
hyperplane will be created based on patterns of information
found in the features, and then used to classify new, unseen
data based on the same features. [2]
For the binary classiﬁcation problem presented here, a
non-linear SVM with an RBF kernel is used. An optimal
kernel scale and box constraint are found by using cross-
validation. The SVM model is trained on the training set,
while the performance is tested on the test set. The threshold
for classiﬁcation normally classiﬁes by separating the classes
at a posterior probability of 50%, but here classiﬁcation
thresholds ranging from 0% to 100% are explored, and the
results are illustrated using a receiver operating characteristic
(ROC) curve, from which a desired model can be selected.
Model 1 is found using a Euclidean distance measure, while
Model 2 is selected intended for implementation in the
WARD system. The obtained performance is evaluated by
calculating the sensitivity (Se), speciﬁcity (Sp), accuracy
(Ac) and positive predictive value (PP).

V. DISCUSSION
When choosing an optimal model for implementation in
the WARD clinical support system, considerations about
the prioritizations of the performance measures must be
made. Here, it is important that the real-time alarms and
the system in general do not lose their credibility with the
medical doctors and nurses due to an excessive amount of
false positives. This will lead to so-called system fatigue,
where the medical staff will take the alarms less seriously,
undermining the beneﬁt of the WARD system, and reducing
the life-saving possibilities that the reduced reaction time to
VT or VF could otherwise provide. To avoid this situation,
the aim is to have as few false alarms as possible, and
therefore, speciﬁcity is prioritized over sensitivity. While
high values of both performance measures is of course
desired, the sensitivity is allowed to decrease to further a
higher speciﬁcity, as a trade-off between the two measures
is typically seen. Furthermore, the uncommonness of VF
in particular plays a role in the prioritization between the
performance measures. For the algorithm to be viable for
implementation in a real clinical support system, a very
high speciﬁcity is needed. As the event occurs so rarely,
many false alarms is a much bigger risk compared to if it
were a more common occurrence. Similarly, the severity
of a VF event increases the demands for the algorithm, as
the alarms become that much more important. Missing a
VT/VF event can have fatal consequences for the patient,
which leads to the considerations about the sensitivity of
the algorithm. Guidance from medical doctors indicates
that as long as the speciﬁcity is very high, a sensitivity
of approximately 70-80% is sufﬁcient for the WARD system.
Model 1 performs well, and is believed to be able to
compete with state of the art algorithms that have been
investigated prior to developing this VT/VF algorithm, while
Model 2 likewise performs excellently for the intended
use. The performances are most likely affected by the
noise present in the signals, which is commonly hard for
classiﬁers to deal with. Furthermore, the addition of VT to
the target class could cause confusion for the models. On
one hand, VT has a morphology that is perhaps more similar
to sinus rhythm (SR) than VF, making it more challenging to
distinguish between SR and VT. On the other hand, the used
features are all frequency-based, and the frequency of VT
is more similar to VF than SR, which may be an advantage
when distinguishing between SR and VT/VF. Finally, the
large class imbalance displayed in Table II creates a very
difﬁcult classiﬁcation problem, and results in a need to
upsample with the SMOTE method in multiple iterations,
meaning that already upsampled data is upsampled again.
The theory is that the data points in the VT/VF-class will be
so similar that not enough variation is obtained to balance
the large variation in the non-VT/VF-class.
When comparing the confusion matrices from Model 1 and
Model 2 given in Figure 3, it is clear to see the prioritizations
at work. While Model 1 in total has fewer misclassiﬁcations
than Model 2, they are quite evenly divided between the two
classes. In contrast, Model 2 is chosen with a small amount
of false alarms in mind, which shows in the very few false
positives, that come at the expense of more false negatives, as
well as a higher total amount of misclassiﬁcations. The real
value of the VT/VF algorithm in the WARD clinical support
system is constituted by the time saved from occurrence
to reaction, which can be life-saving as well as crucial for
the amount of complications that the surviving patient will
experience afterwards. Therefore, though the algorithm may
not detect all VT/VF events, even detecting some of them is
of great value.
VI. CONCLUSION
In this paper, a VT/VF detection algorithm is successfully
developed on public databases. An optimal model (Model
1) is found, as well as an optimal model with regards to
implementation in the WARD clinical support system (Model
2). The developed model is believed to be able to compete
with the state of the art, and though ultimately the rare
occurrence of VT and/or VF episodes creates a very difﬁcult
classiﬁcation problem and the algorithm may not detect all
VT/VF events due to the decreased sensitivity, even detecting
some VT/VF events could be life-saving in the best case and
life-changing in most cases.

C. Reference Algorithm Implementation
C.1
Main Script for VFPred Algorithm
%% VFPred Algorithm
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
%
% DESCRIPTION: This script implements the VFPred algorithm.
%
% AUTHORS: Maria L. Sakhat (s154553) & Lena M. Nilsson (s153554)
%
%
% DATE CREATED: 28−09−20
% DATE MODIFIED: 28−09−20
%
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
%%
clear all
close all
clc
%% ALGORITHM
% Used databases
databases = {'cudb','vfdb'};
% Label condition
testCondition = 0;
% Data segmentation
[Data,fs] = data_segmentation(databases);
% Preprocessing of data
PrepData = preprocessing(Data, fs, databases, testCondition);
clear Data
% Feature extraction
EMDData = feature_extraction(PrepData, fs, databases, testCondition);
clear PrepData
% Feature selection and classification
finalPerformance = selection_classification(EMDData, databases);

function [Data,fs] = data_segmentation(databases)
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
%
% DESCRIPTION: This function loads data and divides it into 5 second
%
segments, adding a label to each segment.
%
% INPUT:
%
o databases: The wanted database name(s)
%
% OUTPUT:
%
o Data: Struct containing each data segment for each subject for each
%
database
%
.(databases): ID of the database
%
.(subjectNo): ID of the subject
%
.(segmentNo): ID of the segment
%
.label: The label for the segment
%
.signal: The signal for the segment
%
.subjectID: The subject ID for the segment
%
.featureLabels: A vector including label conditions
%
o fs: Sampling frequency
%
% AUTHORS: Maria L. Sakhat (s154553) & Lena M. Nilsson (s153554)
%
%
% DATE CREATED: 25−09−20
% DATE MODIFIED: 14−10−20
%
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
%% DATA SEGMENTATION
for db = 1:length(databases)
%% LOAD DATA
DataStruct = loading_data(databases{db});
%% UNWRAP VARIABLES
tm = DataStruct.tm;
signal = DataStruct.signal;
ann = DataStruct.ann;
beatType = DataStruct.beatType;
annType = DataStruct.annType;
fs = DataStruct.fs;
numberOfSubjects = DataStruct.numberOfSubjects;
%% CREATE LABEL VECTOR
% Determine the label types available for the current database
126
==PAGE:152==
C.2. FUNCTIONS
Reference Algorithm
Implementation
if strcmp(databases{db},'cudb')
labelTypes = {'(N';'(VF'};
elseif strcmp(databases{db},'vfdb')
labelTypes = {'(N';'(NSR';'(NOISE';'(VFIB';'(VF'};
end
labelVec = ones(numberOfSubjects,length(signal{1}));
startIdx = [];
endIdx = [];
% Create a label vector for each subject
for i = 1:numberOfSubjects
switch databases{db}
case 'vfdb'
% Determine the type of annotation
annotation = annType{i};
annLocation = ann{i};
% If only one annotation is present
if length(annotation) == 1
% Determine the type of annotation
[~, label] = ismember(annotation,labelTypes);
% Insert the label in the label vector
labelVec(i,annLocation:end) = label;
% If more than one annotation is present
elseif length(annotation) > 1
% Loop through the annotations
for j = 1:length(annotation)
% Add the end index to the annotation locations
annLocation1 = [annLocation; length(labelVec)];
% Determine the type of annotation

% Insert the label in the label vector
labelVec(i,annLocation1(j):annLocation1(j+1)) = label;

elseif beatType{i}(n) == ']'

else

% Set to non−VF

end

% Insert the label in the label vector

signal{i}(startIdx:endIdx,1);

sum(labelVec(i,startIdx:endIdx)==3);

Implementation
sumVF = sum(labelVec(i,startIdx:endIdx)==2);
% If VF samples are present

% Determine the percentage of VF samples in the segment

repelem(sumVF/(sumNonVF+sumVF),10)>=enhancedAnnotationVec;

false(1,10);

if sumVF < sumNonVF
Data.(databases{db}).(subjectNo).(segmentNo).label = 'nonVF';
nonVF = nonVF + 1;
% If the majority of samples are VF, the label is VF
elseif sumVF >= sumNonVF
Data.(databases{db}).(subjectNo).(segmentNo).label = 'VF';
VF = VF + 1;
end
count = count+1;
end
% Move the window
startIdx = startIdx + windowShift;
endIdx = startIdx + windowSize−1;
end
end
end

function [PrepData] = preprocessing(Data, fs, databases, testCondition)
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
%
% DESCRIPTION: This function performs preprocessing of the signal segments.
%
% INPUT:
%
o Data: Struct containing each data segment for each subject for each
%
database
%
.(databases): ID of the database
%
.(subjectNo): ID of the subject
%
.(segmentNo): ID of the segment
%
.label: The label for the segment
%
.signal: The signal for the segment
%
.subjectID: The subject ID for the segment
%
.featureLabels: A vector including label conditions
%
o fs: Sampling frequency
%
o databases: The wanted database name(s)
%
o testCondition: Label condition
%
% OUTPUT:
%
o PrepData: Struct containing each data segment for each subject for each
%
database after preprocessing

.(databases): ID of the database
%
.(subjectNo): ID of the subject
%
.(segmentNo): ID of the segment

% DATE MODIFIED: 14−10−20
%
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
%% DEFINE FILTERS
highpassFreq = 1;
lowpassFreq = 20;
lowpassOrder = 12;
highpassOrder = 1;
nyquist = 0.5*fs;
[bH,aH] = butter(highpassOrder, highpassFreq/nyquist, 'high');
[bL,aL] = butter(lowpassOrder, lowpassFreq/nyquist, 'low');
%% PREPROCESSING
for db = 1:length(databases)
database = databases{db};
for i = 1:numel(fieldnames(Data.(database)))
subjectNo = sprintf('subject%d',i);
for j = 1:numel(fieldnames(Data.(database).(subjectNo)))

==PAGE:157==

% Moving average filter
